# Access Control Policy
## TechNova Ltd

**Document Version:** 1.0
**Date:** 2026-03-25
**Classification:** Internal
**Organization:** TechNova Ltd (London, UK)
**Document Owner:** Chief Information Security Officer
**Last Review:** 2026-03-25
**Next Review Date:** 2027-03-25

---

## 1. Executive Summary

This Access Control Policy establishes the framework for managing user access to TechNova Ltd's information systems and resources in compliance with ISO 27001:2022 standards. The policy applies to all employees, contractors, and third parties with access to company systems. TechNova Ltd is committed to ensuring that access is granted on a least privilege basis, regularly reviewed, and revoked promptly when no longer required.

---

## 2. Policy Scope

This policy applies to:

- All employees of TechNova Ltd
- Contractors and temporary staff
- Third-party service providers and partners
- Systems, applications, and data resources owned or managed by TechNova Ltd
- Remote and on-site access to company infrastructure
- Privileged and administrative accounts

**Exclusions:** Physical access control policies and environmental security measures are covered in separate policies.

---

## 3. Policy Objectives

The objectives of this Access Control Policy are to:

1. Ensure that only authorized users have access to information systems and data
2. Protect against unauthorized access, modification, or disclosure of company information
3. Maintain audit trails of all access activities
4. Comply with ISO 27001:2022 requirements (Controls A.8.1 through A.8.4)
5. Minimize the risk of security incidents related to improper access
6. Establish clear roles and responsibilities for access management
7. Enable timely identification and remediation of access control violations

---

## 4. Roles and Responsibilities

### 4.1 Chief Information Security Officer (CISO)
- Oversees the Access Control Policy and ensures compliance with ISO 27001:2022
- Approves all privileged access management exceptions
- Reviews and authorizes quarterly access reviews
- Ensures adequate resources are allocated for access control implementation

### 4.2 Identity and Access Management (IAM) Team
- Administers user provisioning and deprovisioning
- Manages user identity records and account lifecycle
- Implements and monitors access control systems
- Generates access review reports
- Maintains audit logs and access credentials
- Responds to access-related incidents

### 4.3 System and Application Owners
- Define and document access requirements for their systems
- Approve access requests for their respective systems
- Participate in quarterly access reviews
- Remove access for departing users from their systems

### 4.4 Department Managers
- Request access for their team members
- Approve access requests for their direct reports
- Notify IAM of personnel changes (transfers, departures)
- Validate user access during periodic reviews

### 4.5 All Users
- Use assigned credentials securely
- Protect passwords and authentication factors
- Report suspicious access activity immediately
- Complete mandatory information security training
- Participate in access reviews as requested

---

## 5. User Access Management

### 5.1 Access Request and Authorization

**Process:**
1. **Request Initiation:** Users or managers submit access requests through the designated ticketing system, specifying:
   - Username of the requester
   - Systems or applications requiring access
   - Business justification
   - Required access level (read, write, execute, admin)
   - Duration of access (permanent or temporary)

2. **Approval Chain:**
   - Department manager approval
   - System/application owner approval
   - For privileged access: CISO approval required
   - For external users: Data Protection Officer review

3. **Access Provisioning:** IAM team implements access within 5 business days
4. **Verification:** Access requester confirms access is working correctly
5. **Documentation:** All access grants are logged in the access management system

### 5.2 Access Levels

TechNova Ltd defines the following access levels:

- **Level 1 (Basic User):** Read-only access to general organizational information
- **Level 2 (Power User):** Read and write access to departmental resources
- **Level 3 (Administrator):** System administration and maintenance functions
- **Level 4 (Privileged):** Root, administrative, or superuser access

### 5.3 User Account Lifecycle Management

#### 5.3.1 Account Creation
- Accounts are created only for users with an approved business need
- A unique user identifier (username) is assigned to each user
- Initial passwords are generated randomly and must be changed on first login
- Multi-factor authentication (MFA) is enabled before any production access
- New users complete mandatory security training before account activation

#### 5.3.2 Account Modification
- Changes to access levels require re-authorization through the access request process
- System owners document the business reason for all modifications
- Changes are logged with timestamp and authorized approver details
- Users are notified of access changes via email

#### 5.3.3 Account Termination
- Upon notification of employee departure, access removal begins immediately
- HR provides notification to IAM team at least 48 hours before departure date
- All system access is revoked by end of business on departure date
- All issued credentials, tokens, and physical access items are collected
- Off-boarding checklist is completed and documented
- IT equipment is wiped and reassigned

---

## 6. Privileged Access Management (PAM)

### 6.1 Privileged Account Policy

**Definition:** Privileged accounts are those with elevated access rights including administrative, root, superuser, or system-level access.

**Guiding Principle:** Privileged access is granted on a least privilege basis for the minimum time required to perform job functions.

### 6.2 Privileged Account Management Requirements

1. **Segregation of Duties:**
   - Privileged accounts are separate from regular user accounts
   - Administrators use regular accounts for routine work
   - Administrators use privileged accounts only when performing administrative tasks
   - Privileged accounts are not used for email, internet browsing, or general activities

2. **Account Ownership:**
   - Each privileged account is assigned to a specific individual (no shared accounts)
   - Service accounts used for automated processes are clearly documented
   - No privilege escalation through service account credentials

3. **Authorization and Approval:**
   - All privileged account creation requires documented business justification
   - CISO approval is mandatory for all new privileged accounts
   - Annual re-authorization is required for continuation of privileged access
   - Exceptional access requests are approved on a case-by-case basis

4. **Credential Management:**
   - Privileged credentials are stored in an approved password vault solution
   - Credentials are never shared between users
   - Password changes occur every 90 days for privileged accounts
   - Credential changes are logged and audited
   - Administrative users do not write down passwords

5. **Session Recording and Monitoring:**
   - All privileged account sessions are recorded and logged
   - Session duration is limited to the minimum necessary (default 4 hours)
   - Privileged activities are monitored for anomalous behavior
   - Real-time alerts are configured for critical system changes
   - Recordings are retained for a minimum of 12 months

6. **Just-In-Time (JIT) Access:**
   - Temporary privilege escalation is used where possible
   - Privileges are automatically revoked after session completion
   - Access requests for elevated permissions require documented approval
   - Emergency access procedures allow urgent escalation with after-the-fact authorization (within 24 hours)

### 6.3 Privileged Account Monitoring

- Monthly reports of privileged account activity are reviewed
- Quarterly access reviews specifically validate privileged account necessity
- Real-time alerts are configured for:
  - Unauthorized privilege escalation attempts
  - Access to sensitive data repositories
  - Failed authentication attempts (5+ failures trigger account lockout for 30 minutes)
  - Unusual system changes or configurations

---

## 7. Remote Access Controls

### 7.1 Remote Access Authorization

1. **Eligibility:** Only users with documented business justification may access company systems remotely
2. **Approval:** Department managers and system owners must approve remote access requests
3. **Restrictions:** Remote access is not permitted for users in certain roles (e.g., junior developers) until they complete additional training
4. **Documentation:** All remote access users are recorded in a centralized registry

### 7.2 Virtual Private Network (VPN) Requirements

1. **VPN Requirement:** All remote connections must use the company-approved VPN solution
2. **Certificate-Based Authentication:**
   - VPN access requires both certificate and user credentials
   - Certificates are issued and managed by the IAM team
   - Certificates expire every 12 months and must be renewed
   - Revoked certificates are added to certificate revocation lists (CRLs)

3. **Encryption Standards:**
   - VPN connections use AES-256 encryption
   - TLS 1.2 or higher is the minimum protocol version
   - Weak cipher suites are disabled

4. **VPN Session Management:**
   - VPN sessions expire after 4 hours of inactivity
   - Maximum session duration is 12 hours
   - Users are required to authenticate again after session expiration
   - Concurrent sessions per user are limited to two

### 7.3 Remote Access Device Requirements

Remote devices connecting to company systems must comply with:

- **Device Authentication:** Devices are registered in Mobile Device Management (MDM)
- **Encryption:** Full disk encryption is mandatory (BitLocker, FileVault)
- **Antivirus/Anti-malware:** Current versions of approved security software must be installed
- **Firewall:** Host-based firewall must be enabled and configured
- **Operating System:** Supported versions only; patch level must be within 30 days of latest release
- **Screen Locks:** Automatic screen lock after 15 minutes of inactivity
- **Compliance Monitoring:** MDM tracks compliance; non-compliant devices are denied access

### 7.4 Remote Access Logging and Monitoring

- All VPN access is logged with user identification, timestamp, and connection details
- Logs are retained for a minimum of 12 months
- Weekly reports identify unusual remote access patterns
- Geographic anomalies (impossible travel) trigger security reviews
- Unauthorized access attempts are investigated within 24 hours

### 7.5 Teleconferencing and Collaborative Tools

- Remote meetings and collaboration tools are approved by IT Security
- Video conference accounts require strong authentication
- Recording features are clearly labeled and users consent to recordings
- Screen sharing is restricted during sensitive discussions
- Data transmitted via collaboration tools is classified appropriately

---

## 8. Password and Multi-Factor Authentication (MFA) Requirements

### 8.1 Password Policy

#### 8.1.1 Password Complexity Standards

All user passwords must meet the following requirements:

- **Minimum Length:** 12 characters (14+ characters recommended)
- **Character Requirements:** Passwords must contain:
  - At least one uppercase letter (A-Z)
  - At least one lowercase letter (a-z)
  - At least one numeric digit (0-9)
  - At least one special character (!@#$%^&*-_+=)
- **Password History:** Users cannot reuse their last 12 passwords
- **Password Expiration:** 90 days for regular users; 60 days for privileged accounts
- **Prohibited Content:**
  - No dictionary words
  - No username or reversed username
  - No company name
  - No sequential or repeated characters (e.g., "abc123")

#### 8.1.2 Password Management Practices

- Users do not share passwords under any circumstances
- Passwords are not written down or stored in plain text
- When passwords must be transmitted, secure methods are used (encrypted password vault)
- Default passwords are changed before first use
- Temporary passwords are issued only for emergency situations and must be changed at first login

#### 8.1.3 Password Vault (Privileged Account Manager - PAM)

- A centralized password vault is used for storing sensitive credentials
- Vault access is restricted to authorized administrators
- All vault access is logged and audited
- Vault passwords are rotated every 90 days
- Vault backups are encrypted and stored securely

### 8.2 Multi-Factor Authentication (MFA)

#### 8.2.1 MFA Requirement

MFA is mandatory for:

- All administrative and privileged accounts
- All remote access (VPN, RDP, SSH)
- All access to cloud-based systems (email, collaboration tools, SaaS applications)
- All access to systems containing sensitive or classified data
- All third-party and contractor access

#### 8.2.2 Approved MFA Methods

TechNova Ltd approves the following MFA methods:

1. **TOTP (Time-based One-Time Password):**
   - Authenticator apps (Microsoft Authenticator, Google Authenticator, Authy)
   - Generated codes valid for 30 seconds
   - Backup codes generated and securely stored

2. **Push Notifications:**
   - Mobile app push notifications for transaction approval
   - User must verify device and confirm request
   - Requires registered mobile device

3. **Hardware Security Keys (Preferred for High-Risk Access):**
   - FIDO2-compliant hardware keys (YubiKey, Google Titan)
   - Phishing-resistant authentication
   - Backup keys required and stored separately

4. **SMS/Voice (Fallback Only):**
   - SMS one-time passwords (OTP)
   - Voice call OTP delivery
   - Used only when other methods are unavailable
   - Not approved for high-risk activities

#### 8.2.3 MFA Implementation Standards

- MFA setup is completed during account provisioning
- Users receive training on MFA authentication before first use
- Backup/recovery codes are generated and securely stored by the user
- MFA device/method changes require re-authentication
- Lost or compromised MFA factors are reported immediately to the IAM team
- Account lockout occurs after 5 failed MFA attempts
- MFA bypass is only permitted with CISO authorization and documented exception
- Time-based token synchronization is verified to maintain accuracy

#### 8.2.4 MFA Monitoring

- MFA adoption rates are tracked monthly (target: 100% for in-scope accounts)
- Failed MFA attempts are logged and analyzed for patterns
- Weekly reports identify accounts with disabled or missing MFA
- Non-compliance with MFA requirements results in account suspension until remediated

---

## 9. Access Review Procedures

### 9.1 Access Review Framework

TechNova Ltd conducts regular, systematic access reviews to ensure:
- Access remains appropriate to user roles and responsibilities
- Unnecessary access is identified and removed
- Compliance with access policies is maintained
- No unauthorized access exists

### 9.2 Review Frequency and Scope

#### 9.2.1 Quarterly Access Review (All Users)
- **Frequency:** Every 3 months
- **Scope:** All user accounts and access rights
- **Responsibility:** System owners and department managers
- **Process:**
  1. IAM generates access reports for each system
  2. System owners review and confirm current access is necessary
  3. Department managers validate access for their team members
  4. Discrepancies are resolved within 10 business days
  5. Summary results are reported to CISO

#### 9.2.2 Annual Privileged Account Review (High-Risk Accounts)
- **Frequency:** Annually
- **Scope:** All privileged and administrative accounts
- **Responsibility:** CISO and IAM team
- **Process:**
  1. Comprehensive audit of all privileged accounts
  2. Validation of business justification for continued access
  3. Review of session logs for any suspicious activity
  4. Verification of account ownership and segregation of duties
  5. Management attestation of necessity and appropriateness
  6. Removal or modification of unnecessary privileges

#### 9.2.3 Ad-Hoc Access Reviews
- **Trigger Events:**
  - Organizational restructuring or role changes
  - Security incidents involving access abuse
  - Regulatory requirements
  - Significant system changes
- **Scope:** Affected user populations and systems
- **Responsibility:** CISO with support from relevant system owners
- **Timeline:** Completed within 10 business days of trigger event

### 9.3 Access Review Procedures

**Step 1: Access Report Generation**
- IAM team exports current access lists from all systems
- Reports include: username, assigned role, access level, date granted, business owner
- Data is formatted for review and accountability

**Step 2: Review and Certification**
- System owners or managers review access assignments
- Each access entry is verified as:
  - Still required for job responsibilities
  - Appropriate to user's current role
  - Compliant with segregation of duties
  - Properly documented with business justification

**Step 3: Approval and Sign-Off**
- Reviewers certify their areas with digital signature
- Exceptions or questionable access are documented
- CISO reviews exceptions and approves recommended actions
- Formal approval is documented with date and sign-off

**Step 4: Access Correction**
- IAM team removes or modifies access identified as unnecessary
- Affected users are notified of changes within 5 business days
- Changes are logged and audited
- Compliance confirmation is returned to requestor

**Step 5: Reporting and Documentation**
- Access review results are summarized in monthly security reports
- Metrics tracked:
  - Total accounts reviewed
  - Access changes made
  - Exceptions identified and resolved
  - Compliance percentage
- Reports are retained for audit and regulatory compliance (minimum 3 years)

### 9.4 Access Rights Removal

Immediate access removal occurs when:
- User employment is terminated
- User role or department changes (after manager confirmation)
- User requests access removal
- Security incident investigation determines access should be revoked
- User has not accessed a system for 90 consecutive days (after manager verification)
- MFA requirement is not met within 30 days

Access removal steps:
1. HR or manager notification to IAM
2. IAM verifies user identity and authorization to remove access
3. All access is disabled from all systems
4. Confirmation is provided to requestor within 24 hours
5. Audit log entry is created

---

## 10. Third-Party and Contractor Access

### 10.1 Third-Party Access Policy

**Scope:** External users including contractors, consultants, vendors, and partners requiring access to company systems.

### 10.2 Authorization and Agreements

1. **Contracts:** All third parties must sign a contract that includes:
   - Confidentiality and data protection obligations
   - Security policy compliance requirements
   - Acceptable use policy
   - Audit and monitoring consent
   - Incident reporting requirements

2. **Access Agreements:**
   - Third parties sign a separate Access Authorization Agreement
   - Agreement specifies exact systems and data accessible
   - Duration of access is clearly defined with no auto-renewal
   - Access is revoked within 24 hours of contract termination

3. **Background Verification:**
   - Background checks are conducted based on data sensitivity (BASIC, STANDARD, or ENHANCED level)
   - Verification results are documented and retained
   - High-risk access requires enhanced background verification

### 10.3 Third-Party Account Management

- Third parties are assigned to a separate organizational unit (OU) in the directory
- Accounts are clearly labeled as contractor/temporary accounts
- MFA is mandatory for all third-party access
- Initial passwords are generated by IAM and transmitted securely
- Account review occurs monthly (vs. quarterly for employees)
- Accounts are automatically disabled after contract end date

### 10.4 Third-Party Monitoring

- All third-party account activity is logged separately
- Weekly access and activity reports are generated
- Unusual activities trigger immediate manager notification
- Data access by third parties is monitored and limited to approved repositories
- Periodic audits verify third parties are accessing only authorized resources

---

## 11. Incident Response and Access-Related Violations

### 11.1 Access Violation Reporting

Users must immediately report:
- Suspected unauthorized access
- Credential compromise
- Accounts that cannot be locked or disabled
- Unusual access activity on monitored systems
- Failed authentication attempts indicating potential attacks

**Reporting Methods:**
- Email: security@technova.com
- Phone: +44 (0)20 XXXX XXXX (Internal Security Hotline)
- Incident Management System: Available via internal portal
- Anonymous reporting: Through third-party hotline (details on company intranet)

### 11.2 Investigation Process

1. **Initial Response:** Security team acknowledges report within 2 hours
2. **Containment:** Suspected compromised accounts are locked immediately
3. **Investigation:** Logs are reviewed to determine scope and extent of unauthorized access
4. **Notification:** Affected users and management are notified
5. **Remediation:** Access is removed, credentials are reset, and systems are verified
6. **Review:** Post-incident review identifies root cause and preventive measures
7. **Documentation:** All incidents are documented in the Security Incident Log

### 11.3 Access Policy Violations

Policy violations include:
- Sharing credentials with other users
- Unauthorized access attempts
- Using another person's account
- Circumventing MFA requirements
- Accessing systems without authorization
- Failing to participate in access reviews
- Non-compliance with password or MFA policies

**Consequences:**
- First violation: Formal written warning and mandatory security training
- Second violation: Suspension of system access and disciplinary action
- Third violation: Termination of employment or contract
- Violations involving sensitive data: Immediate investigation and potential legal action

---

## 12. Compliance and Audit

### 12.1 ISO 27001:2022 Alignment

This policy addresses the following ISO 27001:2022 control objectives:

- **A.8.1 User Registration and De-registration:** Account lifecycle management (Section 5.3)
- **A.8.2 User Access Provisioning:** Access request and authorization processes (Section 5.1)
- **A.8.3 Managing Privileged Access Rights:** PAM framework (Section 6)
- **A.8.4 Access Rights Review:** Periodic access reviews (Section 9)

### 12.2 Internal Audits

- Quarterly compliance audits verify policy implementation
- Annual comprehensive access control audits are conducted
- Audit scope includes:
  - Account lifecycle compliance
  - Access request process adherence
  - MFA implementation status
  - PAM system functionality
  - Access review completion and accuracy
  - Third-party access management

### 12.3 External Audits

- Annual third-party security audits review access control procedures
- ISO 27001 certification audits validate compliance with control requirements
- Audit findings are remediated within timeframes agreed with auditors
- Management maintains evidence of remediation

### 12.4 Metrics and Reporting

**Key Performance Indicators (KPIs):**
- Account provisioning time: Target 5 business days
- Access removal time upon termination: Target 24 hours
- Quarterly access review completion: Target 100%
- MFA adoption rate: Target 100% for in-scope accounts
- Policy training completion: Target 100% annually
- Incident resolution time: Target 24 hours for critical incidents

**Reporting Frequency:**
- Monthly: Executive summary to management
- Quarterly: Detailed report to Audit Committee
- Annually: Comprehensive review with Board of Directors

---

## 13. Policy Administration

### 13.1 Policy Ownership and Maintenance

- **Policy Owner:** Chief Information Security Officer
- **Review Cycle:** Annual review minimum; updates as needed based on threats or regulatory changes
- **Approval Authority:** CISO with Board-level security committee oversight
- **Version Control:** All policy versions are tracked with change log
- **Distribution:** Policy is published on the company intranet and provided to all new employees

### 13.2 Training and Awareness

- **Mandatory Training:**
  - All employees complete access control awareness training annually
  - Specific training for system owners on their responsibilities
  - Advanced training for administrators on PAM and secure practices
  - Contractors receive access policy training before account activation

- **Training Methods:**
  - Online training modules (LMS platform)
  - In-person sessions for administrators
  - Quarterly security awareness campaigns
  - Post-incident training for policy violators

### 13.3 Exceptions and Deviations

- **Exception Process:**
  - Exceptions must be requested in writing with business justification
  - CISO approval is required for all exceptions
  - Exceptions have defined expiration dates (maximum 1 year)
  - Exceptions are documented and reported quarterly

- **Emergency Access:**
  - In emergencies, privileged access may be granted without advance approval
  - Emergency access must be documented within 24 hours
  - CISO reviews and approves emergency access after-the-fact
  - Emergency procedures are documented in a separate operational guide

---

## 14. Policy Effectiveness and Review

This policy is effective as of the signature date below and remains in effect until superseded by an updated version. The policy will be reviewed annually and updated as necessary to reflect:

- Changes in technology and security threats
- Regulatory or compliance requirements
- Lessons learned from security incidents
- Feedback from system owners and security teams
- Industry best practices and standards

# SOC 2 Roadmap for Cloud HR Platform — Complete Guidance

## Executive Summary

Your cloud HR platform handles sensitive employee PII for 80 enterprise customers and faces a Fortune 500 audit requirement. This guide walks you through the SOC 2 audit decision framework, scoping criteria, Type 1 vs Type 2 selection, and the five critical controls every HR platform must prioritize.

---

## Part 1: Which Trust Services Criteria to Include

### Mandatory Criteria: Security (CC1–CC9)

All SOC 2 audits must include the **Common Criteria (Security/CC)**, which forms the foundation of your report. For a cloud HR platform handling employee PII, this is non-negotiable:

| Criterion | Applies to HR Platforms? | Why It Matters |
|-----------|-------------------------|---|
| **CC1** Control Environment | Yes | Sets governance tone; required for any organization |
| **CC2** Communication & Information | Yes | Ensures compliance and security policies flow through the organization |
| **CC3** Risk Assessment | Yes | You must formally assess risks to HR data (privacy breaches, unauthorized access, data loss) |
| **CC4** Monitoring Controls | Yes | Auditors verify you monitor system controls continuously |
| **CC5** Control Activities | Yes | Policies, procedures, and technical controls must be documented and enforced |
| **CC6** Logical & Physical Access | Yes | **Critical for HR data** — access to employee records must be restricted and audited |
| **CC7** System Operations | Yes | Incident response, monitoring, disaster recovery are essential |
| **CC8** Change Management | Yes | Code deployments, configuration changes must be controlled |
| **CC9** Vendor Risk | Yes | You depend on cloud infrastructure (AWS/Azure/GCP) and likely SaaS tools; vendor management is expected |

### Optional Criteria: Strongly Recommended for HR Platforms

Beyond the mandatory CC1–CC9, your organization should seriously consider including:

#### **A1 – Availability**
- **Why include it:** HR platforms must be reliable. Outages prevent payroll runs, benefits enrollment, and critical HR operations. Enterprise customers expect high availability.
- **Cost/Benefit:** Moderate effort to document SLAs, monitoring, and DR procedures. High customer value — enterprises assume availability is critical.
- **Recommendation:** **Include in your SOC 2 scope.**

#### **C1 – Confidentiality**
- **Why include it:** Employee PII (SSNs, salary, health plan data, background checks) is extremely sensitive. Confidentiality is one of the top customer concerns.
- **Cost/Benefit:** Requires controls around data encryption at rest and in transit, access logging, and data classification. Moderate effort but essential for trust.
- **Recommendation:** **Include in your SOC 2 scope.**

#### **PI1 – Processing Integrity**
- **Why include it:** HR data must be accurate (payroll miscalculations are costly). Processing integrity ensures data is processed completely, accurately, and securely.
- **Cost/Benefit:** Lower priority than A1 and C1. Adds controls around data validation, audit trails, and error handling.
- **Recommendation:** **Consider including** if you process sensitive transactions (payroll, benefits administration). If you're primarily a HRIS (data storage/query), lower priority.

#### **P1–P8 – Privacy**
- **Why include it:** GDPR, CCPA, and other privacy laws apply to employee data. A comprehensive privacy program increases customer confidence.
- **Cost/Benefit:** Significant effort (privacy policy, DPA management, data subject request procedures). High customer value.
- **Recommendation:** **Include if you process data for employees outside the US or if your customers operate internationally.** At minimum, document your privacy practices even if not formally audited under P1–P8.

### Recommended Scope for Your Organization

**Minimum Scope (Type 1 or Type 2):**
- CC1–CC9 (Security/Common Criteria) — **Mandatory**
- A1 (Availability) — **Strongly recommended**
- C1 (Confidentiality) — **Strongly recommended**

**Extended Scope (adds value for enterprise customers):**
- Include: PI1 (Processing Integrity) and P1–P8 (Privacy)

**Why this scope works:**
- It demonstrates you've thought about reliability, confidentiality, and privacy — the three concerns of enterprise HR customers.
- A1 + C1 + CC1–CC9 can be audited in parallel and share evidence (e.g., access controls support both confidentiality and security).
- Adding P1–P8 signals regulatory awareness and builds trust with international customers.

---

## Part 2: Type 1 vs Type 2 — Which Should You Choose?

### Quick Comparison

| Factor | Type 1 | Type 2 |
|--------|--------|--------|
| **What it covers** | Point-in-time design of controls as of audit date | Operating effectiveness over 6–12 months |
| **Audit timeline** | 4–8 weeks | 6–12 months of operations observed + 4–8 weeks audit |
| **Cost** | Lower (one-time assessment) | Higher (longer audit, more evidence) |
| **Customer perception** | "We had controls designed at one point in time" | "We've proven our controls work consistently" |
| **Validity** | Valid for ~1 year (then refresh needed) | Valid for ~1 year (customers like these more) |
| **Evidence burden** | Moderate — show controls exist in design | High — show controls work repeatedly over the period |

### The Decision Framework for Your Situation

**Go with Type 1 if:**
- This is your first audit and you want a quick proof-of-concept
- You're not yet mature in control operations (lots of manual processes, inconsistent evidence)
- You want to test the audit process before committing to Type 2
- Budget is constrained

**Go with Type 2 if (strongly recommended):**
- Your Fortune 500 customer explicitly asks for Type 2
- You want a report that remains credible for 12+ months without refresh
- You're ready to demonstrate proven operating effectiveness
- You plan to keep the report current — Type 2 reports are more defensible for multi-year customer relationships

### Recommendation for Your Organization

**Choose Type 2.** Here's why:

1. **Fortune 500 Expectation:** Large enterprises typically request Type 2 reports because they prove consistent control operation, not just one-time design.
2. **Long-term Value:** A Type 2 report is valid for ~1 year and much harder to challenge. Type 1 reports often prompt follow-up questions.
3. **Competitive Advantage:** If you're the first of your competitors to achieve Type 2, you gain leverage against vendors offering only Type 1.
4. **Practical Timeline:** Starting your audit 6–9 months before the customer's deadline allows 6 months of operating evidence + 3 months of audit fieldwork.

**Type 2 Implementation Timeline:**
- **Month 0:** Finalize control design, write policies, establish evidence collection
- **Months 1–6:** Operate controls, collect evidence (access reviews, incident logs, change records, vulnerability scans)
- **Month 6:** Engage SOC 2 audit firm, conduct opening meeting
- **Months 7–8:** Auditors perform fieldwork, test controls, request evidence
- **Month 9:** Audit closes, report issued

---

## Part 3: The 5 Most Critical Controls for HR Platforms

Based on the risk profile of cloud HR platforms handling employee PII, these five controls form your foundation. Invest heavily in designing, implementing, and evidencing these first.

### 1. **Access Control & Authorization (CC6.1 / CC6.2)**

**Why it's critical:**
Employee PII (SSNs, salaries, health plan data, background checks, disciplinary records) is among the most sensitive data in an enterprise. A single unauthorized access or privilege escalation can expose data for thousands of employees.

**What to implement:**
- **User access provisioning:** Documented process for granting access based on job role; approval by manager/HR leadership
- **Role-based access control (RBAC):** Employees, HR admins, and executives have different views of data (e.g., HR admin sees all, employee sees only their own)
- **MFA (Multi-Factor Authentication):** Required for all production system access
- **Quarterly access reviews:** HR leadership reviews who has access to sensitive data; sign-off on appropriateness
- **Immediate deprovisioning:** When employees leave, access is revoked within 24 hours

**Evidence for auditors:**
- User access matrix (role → data view mapping)
- Provisioning tickets showing approval chains
- Quarterly access review sign-offs
- Deprovisioning logs (termination → system access removal)
- MFA enrollment records

**Control Owner:** Security Lead / IT Director

---

### 2. **Incident Response & Breach Notification (CC7.2 / CC7.4)**

**Why it's critical:**
If your platform experiences a data breach (ransomware, insider threat, database exposure), your ability to detect, contain, and notify customers is the difference between a minor incident and a catastrophic compliance failure.

**What to implement:**
- **Incident Response Plan:** Documented procedure covering detection, triage, containment, eradication, recovery, and post-incident review. Updated annually.
- **Breach Notification Procedure:** Clear steps for notifying customers, legal, and authorities (varies by jurisdiction — GDPR requires 72 hours, state laws vary)
- **IR Contacts & Escalation:** Defined team members (security, legal, CEO, customer success) and notification chains
- **Tabletop exercises:** Annual simulated breaches to test the plan (documents capture: timeline, decisions, notification sequence, remediation)
- **Incident logging:** All incidents (even suspected/minor) are logged, tracked, and investigated

**Evidence for auditors:**
- Published IR plan with version control and approval date
- Incident logbook (template with incident date, classification, resolution, evidence)
- Breach notification procedure document
- Records of tabletop exercises (participants, scenarios, findings, follow-up actions)
- Examples of real incidents (redacted for confidentiality) showing the plan in action

**Control Owner:** Chief Security Officer / Security Manager

---

### 3. **Data Encryption at Rest & in Transit (CC6.1 / C1.1)**

**Why it's critical:**
Encryption is a preventive control that protects data even if infrastructure is compromised. For HR data, encryption is often a customer requirement and regulatory expectation.

**What to implement:**
- **Encryption at rest:** All databases, backups, and file storage encrypted with strong algorithms (AES-256). Keys managed in a hardware security module (HSM) or AWS/Azure key vault.
- **Encryption in transit:** All data in motion (API calls, database replication, backups) uses TLS 1.2+ over HTTPS
- **Key management policy:** Documented procedures for key rotation (annual), access controls on keys (only need-to-know), and key backup/recovery
- **Evidence of encryption:** System configuration documentation, encryption algorithm verification, key rotation logs

**Evidence for auditors:**
- Data classification policy defining what data is encrypted
- Encryption configuration documentation (AES-256 for at-rest, TLS 1.2+ for in-transit)
- Key management policy with rotation schedule
- Key rotation logs (dates, auditor name, verification)
- System screenshots or technical reports showing encryption status

**Control Owner:** Cloud Architect / Database Administrator

---

### 4. **Vulnerability Management & Patch Management (CC7.1 / CC8.1)**

**Why it's critical:**
Cloud HR platforms depend on many layers: your code, third-party libraries, OS patches, and infrastructure. Unpatched vulnerabilities are a leading cause of breaches. Customers expect you to actively manage this.

**What to implement:**
- **Vulnerability scanning:** Automated weekly/monthly scans using tools (Snyk, Qualys, Nessus) for code, dependencies, and infrastructure
- **Patch management policy:** Documented SLAs for patching critical/high/medium/low vulnerabilities (e.g., critical = within 24 hours)
- **Patch testing & deployment:** Patches tested in staging first; deployment tracked and logged
- **Dependency management:** Regular audits of third-party libraries; updates when security patches released
- **Vulnerability registry:** Documented log of all scanned vulnerabilities, remediation status, and evidence of fixes

**Evidence for auditors:**
- Vulnerability scanning reports (weekly/monthly) showing scans performed
- Patch management policy with SLAs
- Deployment logs showing patches applied and tested
- Dependency audit reports (e.g., npm audit, Snyk reports)
- Vulnerability tracking spreadsheet (ID, severity, detection date, remediation date, status)

**Control Owner:** DevSecOps Engineer / Security Engineer

---

### 5. **Change Management & Code Deployment (CC8.1 / CC8.2)**

**Why it's critical:**
Code changes and system configurations power your HR platform. Uncontrolled changes introduce bugs, security vulnerabilities, and compliance gaps. Auditors expect a formal change process with approval, testing, and audit trails.

**What to implement:**
- **Change request process:** All code changes (features, patches, config updates) must have:
  - Documented change request (what, why, who)
  - Approval by a manager (separation of duties — not approved by the developer)
  - Test results (unit tests, integration tests, security scan results)
  - Deployment log (when, by whom, success/failure)
  - Post-deployment verification (smoke tests, monitoring)
- **Emergency change procedure:** For critical incidents, a streamlined process with retrospective documentation
- **Change log:** Historical record of all deployments with links to change requests and test results
- **Code review:** All code changes peer-reviewed before merge to main branch

**Evidence for auditors:**
- Change management policy document
- Change request template (clearly showing approval chain)
- Sample change requests (10–20 recent examples) with approvals, test results, deployment logs
- Git/deployment logs showing code commit history, reviewer approvals, deployment timestamps
- Post-deployment verification checklists

**Control Owner:** Engineering Manager / DevOps Lead

---

## Supporting Controls — The Context You Need

These five controls are critical, but they operate within a governance context. Don't neglect these supporting elements:

### CC1 – Control Environment
- **Policies & Governance:** Written information security policy, code of conduct, separation of duties framework
- **Training:** Annual security awareness training for all employees; specialized training for developers and admins
- **Accountability:** Security roles defined (CISO, security team, compliance lead) with clear responsibilities

### CC2 – Communication & Information
- **Policy Distribution:** Ensure all employees know policies exist and understand their role (access control, incident reporting, data handling)
- **Compliance attestation:** Employees sign acknowledgment of policies annually

### CC3 – Risk Assessment
- **Annual risk assessment:** Identify key risks (data breach, availability outage, compliance violation); document mitigation strategies
- **Risk register:** Tracking document updated quarterly; evidence of management review and approval

### CC4 – Monitoring Controls
- **System monitoring:** Logging and alerting on access to sensitive data, configuration changes, failed login attempts
- **SIEM/logging infrastructure:** Centralized logging of application, database, and infrastructure events; alerts for suspicious activity

### CC9 – Vendor Risk Management
- **Vendor inventory:** List of all critical vendors (AWS, Azure, payment processors, security tools) with data access / criticality assessment
- **Vendor assessments:** Annually request SOC 2 Type 2 reports from cloud infrastructure providers; review for critical findings
- **SLA tracking:** Document SLAs with vendors; track compliance (uptime, availability, support response)

---

## Getting Started: A 90-Day Action Plan

### Week 1–2: Scoping & Planning
1. **Finalize audit scope** — Confirm CC1–CC9 + A1 + C1 with your Fortune 500 customer
2. **Select Type 2** — Plan for 6–12 month audit timeline
3. **Engage audit firm** — RFQ and contract negotiation with Big 4 or specialized SOC 2 firm
4. **Identify control owners** — Assign responsibility for each control (security, engineering, HR, ops)

### Week 3–4: Policy & Control Design
1. **Draft core policies:** Information Security Policy, Access Control Policy, Incident Response Plan, Change Management Policy (use skill reference `policies.md`)
2. **Define the five critical controls** in detail (use control documentation format from skill reference `controls.md`)
3. **Establish evidence collection process** — Who creates evidence? Where is it stored? (folder structure from skill)

### Week 5–8: Implementation & Hardening
1. **Implement technical controls:** MFA for all users, encryption enabled, vulnerability scanning deployed
2. **Process documentation:** Finalize access review, incident handling, and change request procedures
3. **Evidence collection starts:** Collect baseline evidence (current access matrix, first vulnerability scan, first change log entries)

### Week 9–12: Evidence & Readiness
1. **Organize evidence** — Create folders per criterion (CC1/, CC2/, CC6/, etc.)
2. **Conduct internal readiness assessment** — Dry-run audit with a consultant or internal team
3. **Close gaps** — Address any missing controls or evidence
4. **Notify audit firm** — Confirm Type 2 engagement and agree on fieldwork timeline

## Appendix: Trust Services Criteria at a Glance

**CC1 – Control Environment:** Does the organization have the right tone, governance, and oversight?
- Governance structure, board oversight, management commitment to security

**CC2 – Communication & Information:** Are policies and responsibilities clearly communicated?
- Employee training, policy distribution, communication channels

**CC3 – Risk Assessment:** Does the organization systematically identify and assess risks?
- Risk assessment process, risk register, documented mitigations

**CC4 – Monitoring Controls:** Does the organization continuously monitor control effectiveness?
- System monitoring, logging, alerting, periodic assessments

**CC5 – Control Activities:** Are policies, procedures, and technical controls in place and documented?
- Access controls, change management, incident response, vendor management

**CC6 – Logical & Physical Access Controls:** Is access to systems and data appropriately restricted?
- User provisioning, MFA, access reviews, deprovisioning, physical security

**CC7 – System Operations:** Does the organization detect, respond to, and recover from incidents?
- Incident response, disaster recovery, monitoring, threat detection

**CC8 – Change Management:** Are system changes (code, configuration, infrastructure) controlled and tested?
- Change request process, approval, testing, deployment tracking

**CC9 – Vendor Risk Management:** Does the organization manage risks from vendors and third parties?
- Vendor inventory, assessments, SLA monitoring, CUEC management

**A1 – Availability:** Are systems and data available and performing as expected?
- SLAs, uptime monitoring, disaster recovery testing, incident recovery

**C1 – Confidentiality:** Is sensitive data protected from unauthorized access?
- Data classification, encryption, access controls, retention/destruction

**PI1 – Processing Integrity:** Is data processed completely, accurately, and securely?
- Data validation, error handling, audit trails, completeness checks

**P1–P8 – Privacy:** Does the organization protect personal data in accordance with privacy laws?
- Privacy policy, consent, data subject rights, data transfers, retention

# SOC 2 Audit Roadmap for Cloud HR Platform

## Executive Summary

For your cloud HR platform handling employee PII across 80 enterprise customers, implementing SOC 2 is a strategic imperative. This guide provides a practical framework for planning and executing your first SOC 2 audit.

---

## 1. Trust Services Criteria Selection

### Recommended Framework: AICPA Trust Services Criteria

For a cloud HR platform, you should address the following criteria:

#### **CC (Common Criteria)** - Essential for all organizations
- **CC6.1**: Logical access controls - Authentication, authorization for employee data systems
- **CC6.2**: System monitoring - Detection of unauthorized access attempts
- **CC7.1, CC7.2, CC7.3**: Encryption and transmission security for PII in transit
- **CC7.4**: Secure disposal of data to prevent recovery
- **CC8.1, CC8.2, CC8.3, CC8.4**: Change management and configuration controls

#### **Availability (A)** - Critical for SaaS platforms
- **A1.1, A1.2**: System availability and capacity planning
- **A2.1, A2.2**: System performance monitoring and incident response

#### **Processing Integrity (PI)** - Important for accurate HR data
- **PI1.1, PI1.2**: Accurate and complete data capture
- **PI3.1, PI3.2**: Validation and error handling

#### **Confidentiality (C)** - Highest priority for PII
- **C1.1, C1.2**: Data classification and restriction
- **C6.1, C6.2**: Encryption of data at rest and in transit

#### **Privacy (P)** - Required for PII handling
- **P1.1, P2.1, P3.1, P4.1, P5.1, P6.1, P7.1, P8.1**: Personal information collection, use, retention, disclosure, and individual rights management

### Recommended Approach
Start with **CC (Common Criteria)** and **C (Confidentiality)** as mandatory. **P (Privacy)** is increasingly expected for PII-handling platforms. A (Availability) and PI (Processing Integrity) add significant value demonstrating operational excellence.

---

## 2. Type 1 vs. Type 2: Decision Framework

### Type 1 Audit
- **Scope**: Point-in-time assessment of control design
- **Duration**: Single point in time (typically 1-2 months of testing)
- **When to use**:
  - Organizations without mature controls
  - Quick pathway to SOC 2 certification
  - Budget constraints
  - New platforms or startups

### Type 2 Audit
- **Scope**: Assessment of control design AND operating effectiveness
- **Duration**: Minimum 6-12 month observation period (auditor evaluates actual control operation over time)
- **When to use**:
  - Enterprise customers demanding evidence of control operation
  - Organizations with mature, documented control environments
  - Competitive advantage in market
  - Long-term customer satisfaction

### Recommendation for Your Scenario
**Pursue Type 2 directly** because:
1. Your Fortune 500 client will likely request it (enterprise standard)
2. Your 80 customers expect evidence that controls work consistently
3. Type 2 is more valuable in enterprise procurement processes
4. The 6-12 month observation period allows you to demonstrate maturity
5. Transition cost from Type 1 to Type 2 is high; better to start with Type 2

**Timeline Planning**: Begin planning now for an observation period ending in late 2026 or early 2027, targeting issuance in Q1 2027.

---

## 3. Five Most Critical Controls for HR PII Platforms

### Control 1: Identity and Access Management (IAM)
**Criteria**: CC6.1, CC6.2

**What to implement**:
- Role-based access control (RBAC) restricting HR data by customer tenant
- Multi-factor authentication (MFA) for all system access
- Principle of least privilege documentation
- Quarterly access reviews with documented approvals
- Automated deprovisioning workflows for terminated employees

**Why critical**: Prevents unauthorized access to 80 customers' sensitive employee data. This is the #1 concern for enterprise customers.

---

### Control 2: Data Encryption
**Criteria**: C1.1, C1.2, CC7.1, CC7.2, CC7.3, CC7.4

**What to implement**:
- AES-256 encryption for all data at rest in databases
- TLS 1.2+ for all data in transit
- Key management system (KMS) for encryption key rotation (quarterly minimum)
- Secure key storage separate from application
- Secure data deletion/purge procedures with audit logging
- Hardware security module (HSM) consideration for key storage

**Why critical**: Your customers entrust you with highly sensitive PII (SSNs, compensation, health data, emergency contacts). Encryption demonstrates commitment to confidentiality.

---

### Control 3: Change Management and Configuration Control
**Criteria**: CC8.1, CC8.2, CC8.3, CC8.4

**What to implement**:
- Formalized change management process with approval workflows
- Separation of duties (developer ≠ approver ≠ deployer)
- Change tracking in version control with audit logs
- Automated testing before production deployment
- Rollback procedures documented and tested
- Configuration baselines and drift detection

**Why critical**: Prevents unauthorized or inadequately tested changes that could expose data or break access controls. Enterprise customers demand evidence of disciplined deployment.

---

### Control 4: Audit Logging and Monitoring
**Criteria**: CC6.2, CC7.2, A1.1

**What to implement**:
- Centralized audit logs capturing all access to PII (who, what, when, where)
- Immutable log storage (logs cannot be modified after creation)
- Log retention per data residency/regulatory requirements (typically 2+ years)
- Real-time alerting for suspicious activities (failed logins, bulk data exports, permission changes)
- Regular log reviews and incident investigation procedures
- SIEM integration for correlation and threat detection

**Why critical**: Provides forensic evidence of control effectiveness and enables incident detection/response. Regulators and customers expect this.

---

### Control 5: Data Residency and Isolation (Multi-Tenancy Controls)
**Criteria**: C1.1, C1.2, PI3.1, P1.1

**What to implement**:
- Logical data isolation between customer tenants (database row-level security, schema isolation, or separate databases)
- Documented data segregation architecture
- Testing procedures to verify no data leakage between tenants
- Backup and recovery procedures that respect tenant boundaries
- Data location policies and compliance with customer data residency requirements
- Quarterly penetration testing focused on tenant isolation

**Why critical**: As an SaaS platform, your ability to isolate customer data is fundamental. A breach allowing cross-tenant data access would be catastrophic. Enterprise customers need evidence this is architected correctly.

---

## 4. Implementation Roadmap (12-Month Plan)

### Months 1-2: Assessment & Planning
- Engage SOC 2 auditor for scoping session
- Inventory all systems processing PII
- Document current control environment
- Identify gaps against AICPA criteria
- Determine observation period start date

### Months 3-6: Design & Remediation
- Implement missing controls
- Document all control procedures
- Establish audit logging infrastructure
- Deploy encryption at rest/in transit
- Implement access review processes

### Months 6-12: Observation Period
- Execute controls consistently per documentation
- Maintain detailed evidence of control operation
- Conduct internal audits
- Address any auditor findings
- Build SOC 2 readiness file

### Month 12+: Audit Completion
- Finalize auditor testing
- Receive SOC 2 Type 2 report
- Distribute to customers
- Plan for annual renewal

---

## 5. Resource Considerations

- **Auditor Selection**: Choose Big 4 or specialized SOC 2 firms experienced with SaaS/cloud platforms
- **Internal Resources**: Dedicate security, infrastructure, and operations leaders (15-20% of time minimum)
- **Budget**: Expect $50K-$150K for auditor fees plus internal staffing costs
- **Tools**: Invest in GRC platform, SIEM, and IAM solutions (~$20K-$50K annually)
- **Personnel**: Consider hiring a dedicated compliance/security lead if not already in place

---

## 6. Customer Communication Strategy

Once your SOC 2 Type 2 is achieved:
- Provide report to Fortune 500 client to satisfy procurement requirements
- Use it in sales materials to win new enterprise deals
- Publish security certifications prominently on website
- Include in customer agreements as evidence of security commitment
- Plan annual renewal to maintain certification

---

## Conclusion

Pursuing SOC 2 Type 2 certification is the right strategic move for your HR platform. The five critical controls outlined above—IAM, encryption, change management, audit logging, and multi-tenant isolation—address your greatest risks and customer expectations. Budget 12-18 months for a quality Type 2 engagement and plan for annual renewal audits thereafter.

# SOC 2 Control Documentation

## Control ID: CC6.3-Q-001

**TSC Criterion:** CC6.3 – Logical and Physical Access Controls (Access Termination and Role Change)

**Control Title:** Quarterly Production System Access Reviews

**Control Type:** Detective

**Control Owner:** Security Manager / Access Control Team

**Frequency:** Quarterly

---

## Control Description

The company performs a comprehensive quarterly access review of all production systems to verify that active user access remains appropriate and necessary. This control operates across all critical production infrastructure, cloud environments (AWS, Azure, GCP), SaaS applications, and database systems. During each quarterly cycle, the access control team generates a complete user access roster for all production systems, distributes the roster to system owners and business managers for validation, and documents approval of all active access rights. Users found to have inappropriate, orphaned, or no-longer-required access are flagged, investigated, and removed. The review process ensures that access rights continue to align with current job functions and organizational structure, and identifies access that should have been removed due to employee role changes or terminations that may have been missed in the event-driven offboarding process.

---

## Detailed Control Process

### Quarterly Review Cycle

1. **Access Data Collection** (within first week of each quarter)
   - Access control team exports current user access lists from all production systems
   - Data is consolidated into a standardized access matrix identifying:
     - User name and employee ID
     - System(s) accessed
     - Access type (read, write, admin, or other privilege level)
     - Access grant date
     - Last login date (if available)

2. **Distribution and Review** (weeks 2–3 of each quarter)
   - Access matrix is distributed to designated system owners and business unit managers
   - Reviewers confirm each active user's access is still:
     - Necessary for current job function
     - Appropriate for the user's role and organizational level
     - Consistent with the principle of least privilege
   - Reviewers sign and date the completed matrix (or confirm electronically via system)

3. **Discrepancy Investigation** (weeks 3–4 of each quarter)
   - Access control team investigates all access identified as questionable or flagged during review
   - Verification includes:
     - Confirming employee is still active in the system of record (HRIS)
     - Checking recent login activity to determine if access is actively used
     - Consulting with business manager on business justification

4. **Remediation and De-provisioning** (weeks 4–5 of each quarter)
   - Inappropriate access is removed from all systems within 5 business days of confirmation
   - A record of all access removals (username, system, privilege level, removal date, approver) is maintained
   - De-provisioning is confirmed through system logs

5. **Sign-off and Documentation** (end of quarter)
   - Access control manager prepares a quarterly access review summary
   - Summary is signed off by the CISO or designated executive
   - Signed summary is filed in the access control evidence repository

---

## Evidence

The following artifacts prove this control operates:

1. **Quarterly Access Review Report**
   - Dated export listing all active users and their access rights across production systems
   - Includes collection date, system sources, and data completeness certification
   - Format: CSV, Excel, or GRC tool export with system-generated metadata

2. **System Owner Approval Sign-off**
   - Access matrix signed (digitally or physically) by system owners or delegates
   - Includes reviewer name, title, date of review, and confirmation statement
   - One sign-off per system per quarter
   - Format: Email with timestamp, PDF with signature, or GRC tool approval log

3. **Business Manager Validation**
   - Business unit managers review their department's access rights
   - Includes list of team members, their systems, and approval of appropriateness
   - Format: Email confirmation, spreadsheet sign-off, or system attestation

4. **Access Removal Log**
   - List of all access rights removed during the quarter as a result of the review
   - For each removal: username, system, access type, reason for removal (e.g., role change, no justification), removal date, and approver name
   - Format: Spreadsheet or access control system export

5. **Quarterly Summary Report and Executive Sign-off**
   - Summary memo or report covering:
     - Date range of the review (e.g., Q3 2024: July 1–September 30)
     - Systems in scope
     - Total users reviewed
     - Number of access rights removed
     - Any material findings or exceptions
   - Signed by CISO or Chief Information Security Officer
   - Format: PDF or formal memo with signature

6. **HRIS Comparison Report** (optional but strong evidence)
   - Comparison of active users in production systems against active employees in HRIS
   - Identifies orphaned accounts (users with access but inactive in HRIS)
   - Includes investigation and removal records for orphaned accounts

---

## Test Procedure

### For Type 1 Audits (Point-in-Time Design)

1. **Obtain the most recent quarterly access review report** from the audit date.
2. **Verify control design:**
   - Confirm process documentation (procedure, checklist, or runbook) exists and is current
   - Review evidence that describes the quarterly cadence, systems in scope, and review authority
   - Confirm that system owners and managers are identified as reviewers
   - Verify that removal procedures and timelines are documented

3. **Test design operating effectiveness:**
   - Inspect the most recent access matrix and verify:
     - It is dated and covers all production systems in scope
     - It includes all required data fields (user, system, access level, grant date)
   - Confirm system owner sign-off exists and is dated within the review period
   - Verify business manager attestation or review approval on file
   - Check for CISO or equivalent sign-off on the quarterly summary

4. **Pass/Fail Criteria:**
   - **Pass:** Control design is documented, most recent review is dated and complete, and appropriate reviewers have signed off
   - **Fail:** No review evidence exists, review is undated, or key reviewers have not approved

---

### For Type 2 Audits (Operating Effectiveness Over a Period)

1. **Sample all quarterly reviews within the audit period.**
   - For a 12-month audit period: test all 4 quarters (Q1, Q2, Q3, Q4)
   - For a 6-month period: test all 2 quarters within scope

2. **For each quarterly review, verify:**
   - Access matrix is dated and includes all production systems
   - System owner and business manager sign-offs are present and dated
   - Review period is clearly identified (e.g., Q3 = July–September)
   - No excessive time gaps between data collection, review, and sign-off (should complete within 4–5 weeks)

3. **Test removal follow-through:**
   - For 5–10 access removal entries per quarter, verify:
     - The user's access was actually removed from the target system (via system logs or access list confirmation)
     - Removal occurred within 5 business days of sign-off
     - A business justification was documented (e.g., "user no longer in marketing" or "no access needed")

4. **Verify contemporaneous evidence:**
   - Access matrices and sign-offs should be dated at the time of review, not created retroactively
   - Check system logs to confirm removals align with documented dates

5. **Assess for "rubber stamp" reviews:**
   - Determine if reviews indicate actual examination or are purely perfunctory
   - Evidence of change (access removals, escalations, exceptions) supports genuine review
   - If all reviews show "no changes," inquire about how the team confirmed access remained appropriate

6. **Pass/Fail Criteria:**
   - **Pass:** All quarterly reviews in the period are complete, dated, signed by appropriate reviewers, access removals are timely and documented, and evidence of actual review (not rubber-stamp approval) is visible
   - **Fail:** Reviews are missing, incomplete, unsigned, undated, or access is not removed within documented timelines

---

## Related Policies

- **Access Control Policy** — defines access provisioning, roles, and review procedures
- **Information Security Policy** — establishes security governance framework and access principles
- **Employee Offboarding Policy** — defines immediate access removal upon termination (complements this detective control)

---

## Linked Risks

This control mitigates the following risks:

| Risk | Impact | Mitigation |
|---|---|---|
| Unauthorized access due to role change or termination | High — Former or reassigned employees may retain access to sensitive production systems | Periodic review ensures inappropriate access is detected and removed promptly |
| Privilege creep and excessive access | Medium — Users accumulate access across systems over time beyond job requirements | Quarterly review enforces principle of least privilege and identifies orphaned or excessive rights |
| Undetected account misuse or compromise | Medium — Orphaned or forgotten accounts may be compromised and used for lateral movement | Regular access validation identifies inactive accounts and orphaned system accounts |
| Compliance gap in SOC 2 CC6.5 | Medium — Lack of documented access reviews creates audit findings and regulatory risk | Formal quarterly process with documented evidence demonstrates control and remediation capability |

---

## Control Strengths

- **Detective control:** Provides a periodic "backstop" to catch access issues missed by event-driven termination processes
- **Comprehensive scope:** Covers all production systems and all user types (employees, contractors, service accounts)
- **Documented process:** Clear quarterly cadence with defined roles, timelines, and artifacts
- **Evidence-rich:** Produces contemporaneous sign-offs and removal logs suitable for audit
- **Remediation-oriented:** Includes timely removal procedures and tracking

---

## Common Implementation Considerations

1. **Automation opportunities:**
   - Consider using identity governance (IdG) platforms (Okta, Azure AD, Sailpoint) to automate access matrix generation and reviewer workflows
   - Implement automated escalation if reviewers do not respond within 2 weeks

2. **Orphaned account detection:**
   - Integrate HRIS data to flag users not found in current employee roster
   - Use login analytics to identify accounts with no activity over 90+ days

3. **Service accounts:**
   - Include service accounts and API keys in the review scope
   - Require separate justification for non-human accounts

4. **Documentation standards:**
   - All evidence should include dates, reviewer names (not titles), and system metadata
   - Store evidence in a centralized repository organized by quarter and criterion

5. **Executive engagement:**
   - CISO sign-off on the quarterly summary reinforces importance and accountability

---

## Audit Confidence Statement

When fully implemented and evidenced as described, this control demonstrates a detective mechanism for validating the appropriateness and necessity of access rights across all production systems on a quarterly basis. The combination of system owner, business manager, and executive review, combined with timely remediation tracking, provides reasonable assurance that unauthorized or inappropriate access is identified and removed promptly. This control operates as a complementary procedural control alongside technical access controls and event-driven termination procedures.

# SOC 2 Control Documentation: Access Review Process

## Control Mapping
- **Trust Service Criterion:** CC6.3 - Logical and Physical Access Controls
- **COSO Framework:** Information Security - Access Management
- **Control ID:** CC6.3-ACCESS-REVIEW-001

---

## Control Statement
The company performs quarterly access reviews of all production systems to verify that access is appropriate.

---

## Control Objective
To ensure that logical access to production systems is appropriate, authorized, and remains consistent with business requirements and principle of least privilege through formal, periodic access review and validation processes.

---

## Control Description

### Overview
The organization has established a formal access review process that operates on a quarterly basis. This control requires documented evidence of systematic evaluation of user access rights across all production system environments to verify:
- Access rights remain appropriate to job responsibilities
- Unauthorized or excessive access is identified and remediated
- Segregation of duties is maintained
- Dormant or terminated user accounts are deactivated
- Access aligns with current organizational structure and role assignments

### Key Components

#### 1. Scope Definition
- **Systems Covered:** All production systems and environments, including but not limited to:
  - Production databases
  - Application servers
  - File servers and storage systems
  - Cloud infrastructure and services
  - Administrative and privileged access platforms
  - Network and security infrastructure
- **Users Included:** All user accounts with production system access, including:
  - Permanent employees
  - Contractors and consultants
  - Vendors and third parties
  - Service accounts and system accounts

#### 2. Review Frequency and Timing
- **Schedule:** Quarterly (minimum four times per calendar year)
- **Timing:** Distributed across fiscal/calendar quarters to ensure consistent monitoring
- **Documentation:** All reviews are scheduled and documented in advance

#### 3. Review Procedures
The quarterly access review process includes:

a) **Account Inventory**
   - Compilation of all active user accounts with production system access
   - Identification of access types (administrative, read-only, application-specific, database)
   - Documentation of system and platform coverage

b) **Access Validation**
   - Cross-reference user accounts with current organizational structure and approved personnel lists
   - Verification that access rights align with documented job responsibilities and role definitions
   - Assessment of access appropriateness based on:
     - Job title and function
     - Project assignments and business requirements
     - Need-to-know principle
     - Least privilege principle

c) **Exception Identification**
   - Identification of accounts with:
     - Unnecessary or excessive permissions
     - Access inconsistent with current role
     - Dormant accounts showing no recent activity
     - Orphaned or unassigned accounts
     - Terminated employee accounts still active
     - Separated user access from offboarding procedures

d) **Segregation of Duties Review**
   - Verification that access assignments do not create conflicts of interest
   - Validation that critical incompatible functions remain segregated
   - Documentation of any exceptions with business justification

#### 4. Review Authority and Accountability
- **Responsibility:** System/department owner or designated manager provides access validation
- **Review Ownership:** Information Security or Access Management team leads the review coordination
- **Approval Authority:** Department management and Information Security leadership jointly approve findings and remediation plans
- **Executive Sign-off:** Review results and remediation actions are documented and signed by appropriate management

#### 5. Documentation and Evidence
All quarterly reviews include documented evidence consisting of:
- Access review checklist or standard template
- List of users and their associated access rights per system
- Reviewer's analysis and findings
- Identification of access discrepancies and exceptions
- Business justification for any approved exceptions or segregation of duties violations
- Approval sign-offs from reviewers and management
- Remediation action plans with target completion dates
- Follow-up verification that identified issues were resolved

#### 6. Remediation Process
- **Non-Compliance Identification:** Access rights that do not meet criteria are formally documented
- **Remediation Action Plan:** For each identified discrepancy, specify:
  - Nature of the access issue
  - Required action (modification, removal, or approval of exception)
  - Owner responsible for remediation
  - Target completion date
- **Implementation Verification:** Follow-up review confirms access modifications were completed
- **Escalation:** Unresolved issues are escalated to leadership for resolution

#### 7. Exception Management
- **Exception Documentation:** Any approved exceptions to standard access policies are:
  - Explicitly documented with business justification
  - Reviewed and approved by appropriate authority
  - Subject to the same quarterly review as standard access
  - Time-limited and subject to periodic re-evaluation

#### 8. Reporting and Communication
- **Results Documentation:** Quarterly review results are compiled into formal reports that include:
  - Executive summary of access environment
  - Summary of findings and exceptions
  - Remediation status from previous reviews
  - Recommendations for improvements
- **Distribution:** Reports are provided to:
  - Executive leadership and audit stakeholders
  - Department and system owners
  - Compliance and risk management functions

---

## Supporting Controls and Dependencies
- **AC-2: Account Management** - User provisioning and deprovisioning procedures
- **AC-3: Access Enforcement** - System controls that implement access decisions
- **AU-6: Audit Review, Analysis, and Reporting** - Log analysis and access monitoring
- **CA-7: Continuous Monitoring** - Supplementary system monitoring between reviews
- **PS-4: Personnel Termination** - Timely deactivation of terminated user access

---

## Evidence and Testing Procedures

### Required Documentation
1. Quarterly access review plans and schedules
2. Access review work papers for each review cycle:
   - Complete user access listings
   - Reviewer analysis and validation notes
   - Exception logs with business justification
   - Management approval records
3. Remediation tracking logs for identified deficiencies
4. Follow-up verification evidence that remediation was completed
5. Management sign-off and certification on review completion

### Audit Testing Approach
Auditors will:
1. Verify execution of quarterly reviews (4 reviews annually)
2. Select a sample of access rights for detailed testing
3. Confirm that access is appropriate to user role and responsibilities
4. Verify timely remediation of identified exceptions
5. Assess adequacy of exception justification and approval
6. Confirm segregation of duties is maintained

---

## Effectiveness Criteria
The control is considered effective when:
- Quarterly reviews are consistently performed within the defined schedule
- Review documentation is complete and contains sufficient detail for independent assessment
- Identified access issues are investigated and documented
- Remediation actions address root causes of access discrepancies
- There is no material instance of inappropriate, unauthorized, or excessive access remaining unaddressed
- Exception access is formally approved, documented, and subject to periodic re-evaluation
- Review results are properly reported and communicated to appropriate stakeholders
- Historical trends show improvement in access control posture over time

---

## Reliance and Complementary Controls
This control operates in conjunction with:
- System-based access controls that enforce approved access decisions
- Identity and access management infrastructure
- Continuous monitoring and logging systems that provide supplementary assurance
- Change management processes that document access modifications
- Offboarding procedures that ensure timely removal of separated user access

---

## Limitations and Assumptions
- This control provides reasonable, but not absolute, assurance of access appropriateness
- Effectiveness depends on the accuracy and completeness of system access reports and user role definitions
- The quarterly frequency provides periodic assurance; continuous monitoring systems provide supplementary real-time verification
- Manual review procedures are subject to human error and bias; system controls provide additional verification

---

### Appendix B: Risk Assessment for Control Failure
- **Risk:** Unauthorized access to production systems allows data compromise or system damage
- **Impact:** Data breach, regulatory non-compliance, reputational damage, operational disruption
- **Likelihood without control:** High (access rights proliferate without periodic validation)
- **Mitigation provided by control:** Quarterly validation reduces risk of prolonged unauthorized access
