# PCI DSS Compliance Assessment: E-Commerce Store with Stripe.js

## Executive Summary
Based on the described payment processing architecture, your organization qualifies for **SAQ A-EP (SAQ A, Expanded)** under PCI DSS 3.2.1 (or equivalently SAQ A as revised in PCI DSS 4.0). This is the most favorable assessment type for merchant organizations.

---

## 1. SAQ Type Determination: SAQ A-EP

### Qualification Criteria Met:
Your e-commerce store meets all requirements for SAQ A-EP qualification:

1. **Hosted Payment Fields (HPF) Compliance**: You use Stripe.js hosted payment fields, which is Stripe's approved hosted solution that redirects cardholder data submission directly to Stripe's secure servers.

2. **No Direct Card Data Access**: Your servers never touch PANs (Primary Account Numbers). Card data flows directly from the customer's browser to Stripe without passing through your infrastructure.

3. **PCI-Compliant Payment Service Provider (PSP)**: Stripe is a fully PCI DSS Level 1 certified service provider, meeting the highest certification standard.

4. **Stored Data Scope**: You store only non-sensitive data:
   - Customer names and emails (non-payment card data)
   - Order history (non-payment card data)
   - Stripe payment tokens (substituted data, not PANs)
   - No full card numbers, expiration dates, or CVV codes stored

5. **Transaction Volume**: At 15,000 transactions annually, your volume does not disqualify you from SAQ A-EP (which has no transaction count limitations).

### SAQ A-EP Definition:
SAQ A-EP applies to merchants using a hosted payment page, where:
- The payment page is hosted by a PCI-certified service provider
- The merchant does not transmit or store cardholder data
- The merchant's involvement is limited to redirecting customers to the payment processor

---

## 2. ROC Requirement: NOT REQUIRED

### Justification:
**You do NOT require a Report on Compliance (ROC).**

A ROC is required only for:
- SAQ B merchants (and higher complexity assessments)
- Merchants processing more than a defined transaction threshold AND meeting specific complexity criteria
- Merchants with higher-risk payment processing architectures

Your situation:
- **SAQ A-EP merchants are explicitly exempt from ROC requirements** in PCI DSS guidelines
- Your organization may complete a self-assessment questionnaire (SAQ A-EP) instead
- The SAQ serves as your primary compliance documentation tool

### Alternative Compliance Documentation:
- Complete the PCI DSS Self-Assessment Questionnaire (SAQ) Type A-EP
- Submit attestation of compliance to your acquiring bank/payment processor
- No third-party auditor (QSA) assessment is required
- No formal ROC is necessary

---

## 3. CDE (Cardholder Data Environment) Scope Analysis

### What IS In Your CDE:
1. **Customer Names and Emails** - These are stored and are payment-related metadata, but they are NOT cardholder data as defined by PCI DSS (non-sensitive personal information).

2. **Order History** - Non-payment card data; supports transactions but contains no actual payment credentials.

3. **Stripe Payment Tokens** - These are substitute payment instruments issued by Stripe. Tokens represent payment authorization without containing the underlying card data and are generally considered safe to store under PCI DSS.

### What IS NOT In Your CDE:
- **Full card numbers (PANs)**
- **Expiration dates**
- **CVV/CVC codes**
- **PIN blocks**
- **Magnetic stripe data**
- **Any sensitive authentication data (SAD)**

### CDE Scope Determination:
**Your CDE scope is MINIMAL and SIMPLIFIED**, which includes:

1. **Web Application Layer**: The e-commerce application that:
   - Collects customer information (name, email)
   - Records order history
   - Manages Stripe tokens
   - Does NOT handle raw card data submission (Stripe.js handles this)

2. **Data Storage Systems**: Database servers that store:
   - Customer names and emails
   - Order records
   - Stripe payment tokens
   - Transaction logs

3. **Network Perimeter**: Limited to systems necessary to support the above (web servers, application servers, database servers for payment-related data).

### What IS EXCLUDED From CDE Scope:
- **Customer service systems** (unless they access stored tokens)
- **Marketing systems** (unless they contain payment identifiers)
- **Analytics systems** (unless they contain cardholder data)
- **Backup/archive systems** (containing customer PII only, not payment card data)
- **Development environments** (if properly segregated from production)

### CDE Justification:
Because you:
- Use a hosted payment field solution (Stripe.js)
- Never process raw PANs in-house
- Store only tokens (not sensitive card data)
- Maintain proper segmentation of payment-related systems

Your CDE is dramatically smaller and simpler than traditional merchants who process cardholder data directly.

---

## 4. Compliance Obligations and Best Practices

### Core PCI DSS Requirements for Your SAQ A-EP Status:

**Still Required (Subset):**
1. **Requirement 1**: Maintain a firewall configuration
2. **Requirement 2**: Do not use vendor defaults for passwords and security parameters
3. **Requirement 3**: Protect stored cardholder data (your tokens)
4. **Requirement 4**: Encrypt cardholder data transmission (HTTPS)
5. **Requirement 6.2**: Install security patches promptly
6. **Requirement 6.5.10**: Broken authentication remediation
7. **Requirement 8**: Maintain user access controls
8. **Requirement 10**: Maintain audit logs and monitoring
9. **Requirement 12.1**: Maintain a security policy

**Simplified or Not Applicable:**
- Requirement 5-9 (partially or fully reduced scope)
- Cryptography and key management (reduced scope)
- Network segmentation (simplified)

### Annual Compliance Submission:
- Complete SAQ A-EP questionnaire annually
- Submit attestation to your acquiring bank
- Document compliance with simplified requirement set
- No formal external audit required (ROC not needed)

### Recommended Best Practices (Beyond Minimum PCI Requirements):
1. **Regular Security Assessments**: Conduct internal security reviews quarterly
2. **Token Management**: Implement proper token lifecycle management with Stripe
3. **Access Controls**: Restrict employee access to payment tokens (least privilege)
4. **Monitoring**: Log all access to systems handling payment tokens
5. **Incident Response**: Maintain incident response plan for potential data breaches
6. **Employee Training**: Annual security awareness training covering token handling
7. **Vendor Management**: Maintain agreements with Stripe confirming PCI compliance
8. **Payment Token Segmentation**: Segregate systems handling tokens from general systems
9. **HTTPS Enforcement**: Enforce TLS 1.2+ across all payment-related communications
10. **Secure Development**: Follow secure coding practices in e-commerce application

---

## 5. Summary Table

| Aspect | Answer |
|--------|--------|
| **SAQ Type** | SAQ A-EP (Hosted Payment Page) |
| **ROC Required** | NO |
| **Merchant Tier** | Self-Assessment Only |
| **CDE Scope** | Minimal - Limited to systems storing customer names, emails, order history, and payment tokens |
| **Sensitive Data Handling** | None (PANs not stored or processed) |
| **Key Qualification** | Using Stripe.js hosted payment fields (certified solution) |
| **Annual Compliance** | SAQ A-EP self-assessment + bank attestation |
| **External Audit** | Not required |
| **Complexity Rating** | **Low** - Simplified compliance program |

---

## 6. Important Caveats and Verification Recommendations

1. **Confirm Stripe.js Implementation**: Verify that your implementation uses ONLY hosted payment fields and that no custom JavaScript handles raw card data.

2. **Token-Only Storage**: Confirm that your systems store ONLY Stripe payment tokens and never intercept or store PANs, expiration dates, or CVV codes.

3. **Validation with Acquiring Bank**: Present this assessment to your acquiring bank/payment processor for official confirmation, as they may have specific requirements.

4. **Validation with Stripe**: Confirm with Stripe that your implementation meets their integration standards for SAQ A-EP qualification.

5. **Regular Reassessment**: If your architecture changes (e.g., processing more payment data types, implementing new payment methods), reassess your SAQ type.

6. **PCI DSS Version**: This assessment is based on PCI DSS 3.2.1 standards. Ensure alignment with your acquiring bank's required PCI DSS version.

---

## Conclusion

Your e-commerce store's use of Stripe.js hosted payment fields places you in an **optimized PCI compliance position**:

- ✅ **SAQ Type**: A-EP (simplest self-assessment questionnaire)
- ✅ **ROC**: Not required
- ✅ **CDE Scope**: Minimal and manageable
- ✅ **Compliance Cost**: Low (self-assessment vs. third-party audits)
- ✅ **Operational Burden**: Minimal (simplified requirement set)

This is the preferred architecture for merchants seeking to minimize compliance overhead while maintaining strong security for cardholder data.

# PCI DSS v3.2.1 to v4.0.1: Comprehensive List of New Mandatory Requirements (Effective March 31, 2025)

**Prepared for:** QSA Assessment
**Reference Standard:** PCI DSS v4.0.1 (June 2024)
**Compliance Deadline:** March 31, 2025
**Document Date:** March 25, 2026

---

## Executive Summary

On **March 31, 2025**, all "future-dated" requirements from PCI DSS v4.0 transitioned from best practice to **mandatory compliance requirements**. These requirements represent significant security enhancements across eight of the twelve PCI DSS requirements, with particular impact on authentication, e-commerce payment security, logging infrastructure, and access control.

Organizations that were previously PCI DSS v3.2.1 compliant must now demonstrate compliance with these new mandatory controls in their next assessment. **Non-compliance with any of these requirements is a finding that must be remediated before passing validation.**

This document provides a comprehensive breakdown of the 22 most important new mandatory requirements, organized by control area and implementation priority.

---

## Section 1: Most Critical Changes — Highest Business Impact

### 1.1 **Requirement 8.4.2: Multi-Factor Authentication (MFA) for ALL CDE Access**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 required MFA only for non-console administrative access and remote access from outside the network
- v4.0.1 **extends MFA to ALL access into the CDE** — including access from internal corporate networks

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- MFA required for every user accessing any CDE system, regardless of physical location or network origin
- Applies to system administrators, database administrators, application support staff, developers, and any personnel interacting with CDE systems
- Network segmentation and VPN access alone no longer satisfy this requirement
- At least two of the following authentication factors required:
  - Something you know (password/passphrase)
  - Something you have (hardware token, smart card, mobile device)
  - Something you are (biometric: fingerprint, face recognition)

**Implementation Priority:** CRITICAL
**Common Gaps:** Organizations assuming VPN + internal network = sufficient; MFA only deployed for remote/admin access; weak MFA solutions (SMS-based only, without backup factors)

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- MFA deployment configuration across all CDE access points
- Logs demonstrating MFA authentication challenges and successes
- Policy and procedure documentation requiring MFA for all CDE access
- Training records showing personnel understand MFA requirement

---

### 1.2 **Requirement 6.4.3: Payment Page Script Inventory and Integrity Protection**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 had limited requirements for script management
- v4.0.1 **mandates comprehensive inventory and protection of all scripts** on payment pages to prevent web skimming attacks (e.g., Magecart)

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- **Complete Inventory:** Identify every script (JavaScript, third-party widgets, CDN-hosted libraries, tracking pixels, analytics) loaded in the consumer's browser on payment pages
- **Authorization:** Document business justification for every script — who authorized it, why it's needed, date approved
- **Integrity Protection:** Deploy change detection mechanisms:
  - Content Security Policy (CSP) headers with script-src restrictions
  - Subresource Integrity (SRI) validation with hash verification
  - Network tamper detection capabilities
  - Alternative: equivalent integrity protection approved by QSA

**Implementation Priority:** CRITICAL (if e-commerce payment processing)
**Common Gaps:** Outdated script inventory; unauthorized scripts (analytics, ads, third-party tags) without documented business case; CSP/SRI not implemented; reactive detection only (discovering tampering after attack)

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Documented script inventory with approval dates and business justifications
- CSP/SRI header configuration and testing results
- Change detection mechanism configuration and alerting logs
- Evidence of script integrity verification testing

---

### 1.3 **Requirement 11.6.1: Change and Tamper Detection for Payment Pages**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 had no explicit requirement for payment page modification detection
- v4.0.1 **mandates active detection and alerting** when payment pages are tampered with

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- Deploy automated change detection mechanisms that alert personnel when:
  - HTTP headers are modified (e.g., CSP, X-Frame-Options, HSTS)
  - Payment page HTML content changes unexpectedly
  - JavaScript source code or attributes are modified
  - DOM elements or form structure alter
- Alert frequency: At minimum every 7 days (or higher frequency defined by targeted risk analysis)
- Real-time alerting required for critical changes
- Documented response procedures for tamper alerts

**Implementation Priority:** CRITICAL (if e-commerce payment processing)
**Common Gaps:** Manual periodic reviews only (not automated); infrequent or missing tamper detection; alerts configured but not reviewed; no response playbook for tamper incidents

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Tamper detection tool configuration and system logs
- Alert logs demonstrating detection of changes (including test cases)
- Documentation of alert frequency and response procedures
- Incident logs showing tamper detection and remediation

---

### 1.4 **Requirement 10.4.1.1: Automated Log Review Mechanisms**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 allowed manual daily log review
- v4.0.1 **mandates automated log review — manual review alone is insufficient**

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- Automated log aggregation and analysis system (SIEM, log management platform, or equivalent)
- Configured alert rules that detect:
  - Suspicious authentication attempts (failed logins, brute force)
  - Privilege escalation attempts
  - Unauthorized access attempts
  - Data access anomalies
  - Configuration changes to security controls
  - Critical system errors
- Automated alerts must notify personnel promptly (not post-review)
- Logs retained for minimum 1 year (3 months online/searchable)

**Implementation Priority:** HIGH
**Common Gaps:** SIEM deployed but alerting rules not configured; alerts generated but not reviewed; manual log reviews continuing but no automated system; retention periods inadequate

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- SIEM or log management system configuration and architecture
- Alert rule documentation with explanation of detection logic
- Alert notification logs showing real-time detection and response
- Incident response records tied to automated alerts

---

## Section 2: Access Control & Authentication Enhancements

### 2.1 **Requirement 7.2.4: User Account and Privilege Reviews (6-Month Cycle)**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 required review but frequency was less prescriptive
- v4.0.1 **mandates explicit 6-month review minimum** for all active user accounts and access privileges

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- At minimum every 6 months, conduct formal review of:
  - All active user accounts in CDE systems
  - Assigned access privileges and permissions
  - Accounts no longer needed (disabled/removed)
  - Privilege escalations or role changes since last review
- Document review results: who reviewed, date, accounts validated, accounts removed
- Management approval of findings and remediation

**Implementation Priority:** HIGH
**Common Gaps:** Annual reviews only; no documented access reviews; dormant accounts remain active; privilege creep not detected; reviews performed but not formally documented

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Documented access review schedules and completion records (at least 2 cycles in past 12 months)
- Account listings reviewed, with annotations showing validation status
- Evidence of disabled/removed accounts
- Management sign-off on review findings

---

### 2.2 **Requirement 7.2.5 & 7.2.5.1: Application/System Account Management**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 had general account management requirements
- v4.0.1 **explicitly mandates management of system/application accounts** and review of their privileges every 6 months

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- Identify and document all system/application accounts (service accounts, application IDs, batch user IDs, database service accounts)
- Manage these accounts with the same rigor as user accounts:
  - Defined purpose and business owner
  - Least-privilege principle (minimal permissions needed)
  - Password/credential policies enforced
  - No hardcoded credentials in scripts or source code (Req 8.6.2)
- At minimum every 6 months, review and validate the necessity and permissions of each system account
- Document management and review procedures

**Implementation Priority:** HIGH
**Common Gaps:** System accounts not inventoried; shared credentials; hardcoded passwords in batch scripts; no periodic review; service accounts with excessive privileges; inactive accounts not disabled

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- System account inventory with documented business justification
- Account privilege documentation and review records
- Code review results confirming no hardcoded credentials
- Evidence of 6-month privilege reviews

---

### 2.3 **Requirement 8.3.6: Password Change Frequency for Non-MFA Users**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 had general password change requirements
- v4.0.1 **tightens requirement to 90-day maximum** for users without MFA

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- For any user account that has NOT implemented MFA:
  - Password/passphrase must be changed at minimum every 90 days
  - This accelerates the change cycle to encourage MFA adoption
- Users with MFA implemented may have longer change intervals (per Customised Approach with TRA)
- Automated enforcement via password management systems preferred

**Implementation Priority:** MEDIUM
**Common Gaps:** 180-day change cycles still in place; no automated enforcement; users without MFA not tracked separately; change frequency policy not documented

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Password policy documentation specifying 90-day requirement
- System logs showing password change enforcement
- Audit trail of password changes for sample of non-MFA users

---

### 2.4 **Requirement 8.6.1: Interactive System/Application Account Management**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 did not explicitly address interactive system accounts
- v4.0.1 **mandates formal management and protection** of system/application accounts capable of interactive login

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- Identify system/application accounts that can be used interactively (not just for batch/scheduled processes)
- Manage these accounts with explicit controls:
  - Documented purpose and business justification
  - Access control policies defining who can use the account
  - Audit logging of all interactive sessions
  - MFA or equivalent strong authentication if CDE access
  - Disable accounts no longer needed
- Review procedures to ensure accounts remain necessary

**Implementation Priority:** MEDIUM
**Common Gaps:** Shared interactive accounts without audit trail; no documented approval for who can access; system accounts used by multiple people; lack of logging for interactive sessions

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- System account inventory identifying interactive accounts
- Policy documentation controlling access to these accounts
- Session logging and audit trails
- Evidence of account review and validation

---

### 2.5 **Requirement 8.6.2: No Hardcoded Credentials in Code**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 had general secure coding requirements
- v4.0.1 **explicitly prohibits hardcoded passwords** in scripts, applications, and source code

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- Scan all application source code, configuration files, and scripts for embedded credentials
- Implement Secure Software Development Lifecycle (SDLC) controls:
  - Code review processes that check for hardcoded secrets
  - Static code analysis tools configured to detect credentials
  - Pre-commit hooks preventing password/key commits to repositories
- Use secure credential management instead:
  - Environment variables
  - Secrets vaults (HashiCorp Vault, AWS Secrets Manager, etc.)
  - Configuration management systems
  - Key management systems (HSM)
- Remediate existing hardcoded credentials: rotate passwords/keys, update code, redeploy

**Implementation Priority:** HIGH
**Common Gaps:** Database passwords in connection strings; API keys in scripts; SSH keys embedded in config files; legacy systems with hardcoded credentials; no code scanning tools in place

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Source code repository scan results for credentials
- SDLC documentation and code review evidence
- Static code analysis configuration and reports
- Credential management system configuration and usage logs

---

## Section 3: Software Development & Vulnerability Management

### 3.1 **Requirement 6.3.2: Software Bill of Materials (SBOM)**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 did not explicitly require SBOM
- v4.0.1 **mandates SBOM maintenance** for bespoke and custom software

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- For all custom-developed, bespoke, or in-house software used in the CDE:
  - Create and maintain a Software Bill of Materials (SBOM) documenting:
    - All direct and transitive software dependencies
    - Library/component versions used
    - License information
    - Known vulnerabilities in components
- SBOM formats: SPDX, CycloneDX, or equivalent
- Update SBOM with each software release
- Use SBOM to identify vulnerable components and prioritize patching
- Integrate SBOM generation into SDLC (build pipelines)

**Implementation Priority:** HIGH (if custom software in CDE)
**Common Gaps:** No SBOM generated; dependencies not tracked; open-source components not inventoried; SBOM not updated with releases; vulnerabilities in components not identified

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- SBOM documentation for all in-scope custom applications
- Evidence of SBOM generation tools and processes
- Vulnerability tracking linked to SBOM components
- SBOM update records with application releases

---

### 3.2 **Requirement 5.4.1: Automated Anti-Phishing Technical Solutions**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 had no explicit phishing protection requirement
- v4.0.1 **mandates automated technical solutions** to detect and protect against phishing

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- Deploy at least one automated technical solution designed to detect and prevent phishing attacks, such as:
  - Email security gateway with anti-phishing/URL sandboxing
  - DNS filtering (blocking known malicious domains)
  - Email authentication (DMARC, DKIM, SPF enforcement)
  - User authentication bypass prevention (anti-credential harvesting)
  - Browser-based anti-phishing extensions (managed/enforced)
- Solution must protect both email and web-based phishing attempts
- Alerts must be reviewed regularly
- Security awareness training should complement technical controls

**Implementation Priority:** HIGH
**Common Gaps:** Email security present but not configured for phishing; no URL filtering; no DNS filtering; DMARC/DKIM/SPF not enforced; user training only (no technical solution)

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Email security gateway configuration and phishing detection logs
- DNS filtering or email authentication configuration
- Logs showing detected/blocked phishing attempts
- Alert review records and remediation evidence

---

### 3.3 **Requirement 5.3.3: Anti-Malware Scanning of Removable Media**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 did not explicitly require scanning of removable media
- v4.0.1 **mandates anti-malware scans** on removable electronic media

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- Anti-malware solution must scan removable media (USB drives, external hard drives, memory cards, etc.)
- Scanning must occur:
  - Upon insertion/connection to CDE systems
  - On a scheduled basis (weekly or per policy)
- Controls to prevent unauthorized removable media from connecting (policy-based, hardware-based)
- Documented procedure for handling infected media
- Logging and alerting on detected malware

**Implementation Priority:** MEDIUM
**Common Gaps:** Anti-malware focuses on endpoints/servers but not removable media; no scheduled scans; no policy preventing media use; infected media discovered but not logged; no documented procedures

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Anti-malware solution configuration for removable media scanning
- Scan logs showing periodic scans and detections
- Policy documentation on removable media use
- Incident logs showing detected/remediated threats

---

## Section 4: Data Protection & Cryptography

### 4.1 **Requirements 3.3.2 & 3.3.3: Pre-Authorization SAD Encryption (Issuers/Support Services)**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 prohibited SAD storage after authorization
- v4.0.1 **encrypts SAD stored BEFORE authorization** (for issuers and issuer-support entities only)

**Applicability:** This requirement applies **only to payment card issuers and entities supporting issuing services**. Most merchants and acquirer-side service providers are NOT in scope for this requirement.

**What Must Be Implemented (if applicable):**
- Sensitive authentication data (full magnetic stripe, CAV2/CVV2/CVC2/CID, PIN/PIN block) stored before authorization must be encrypted
- Encryption uses strong cryptography (AES-256 or equivalent)
- Encryption keys managed per Requirement 3.6–3.7 (key management lifecycle)
- Post-authorization, SAD remains prohibited from storage

**Implementation Priority:** N/A for most organizations; CRITICAL for issuers

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Encryption configuration for pre-auth SAD storage
- Key management documentation
- Testing confirming SAD unreadable in storage

---

### 4.2 **Requirement 4.2.1.1: Inventory of Trusted Keys and Certificates**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 required certificate management but not explicit inventory
- v4.0.1 **mandates documented inventory** of all trusted keys and certificates

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- Maintain a comprehensive inventory of all cryptographic keys and digital certificates used for CDE data protection:
  - Encryption keys (for PAN and CHD at rest)
  - Keys for data in transit (TLS/SSL certificates, API keys)
  - Key-encrypting keys (KEKs)
  - Signing keys
- For each key/certificate, document:
  - Purpose and system/service using it
  - Type and algorithm (RSA-2048, AES-256, ECDSA-256, etc.)
  - Validity dates (issuance, expiration)
  - Key custodian
  - Retirement/rotation status
- Regular review to identify expired or compromised certificates
- Proactive renewal procedures (before expiration)

**Implementation Priority:** HIGH
**Common Gaps:** Keys not inventoried; expired certificates in use; no certificate expiration tracking; no centralized key/certificate management; key rotation not planned

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Documented key and certificate inventory
- Certificate expiration tracking and renewal records
- Key rotation schedule and execution logs
- Review records confirming key/certificate validity

---

## Section 5: Compliance Program & Governance

### 5.1 **Requirement 12.3.2: Targeted Risk Analysis (TRA) for Customised Approach**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 had no formal risk analysis requirement
- v4.0.1 **mandates Targeted Risk Analysis (TRA)** for each customized control implementation

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- For any PCI DSS requirement where an organization opts for the Customised Approach (alternative control design):
  - Conduct a formal TRA documenting:
    - Risk description (what is the security objective?)
    - Why the defined approach cannot be implemented
    - How the customized control achieves the PCI DSS objective
    - Evidence of control effectiveness
    - Residual risk assessment
    - Senior management approval
    - Annual review and revalidation date
  - TRA must be documented and available to QSA/ISA
  - Customized controls assessed by QSA using Customised Approach Test Plan (CATP)

**Implementation Priority:** MEDIUM (if using Customised Approach)
**Common Gaps:** No TRA process documented; TRA not formally approved; customized controls implemented without risk analysis; TRA not updated annually; QSA not engaged for CATP assessment

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Documented TRA for each customized control
- Risk assessment, objective alignment, and effectiveness evidence
- Senior management approval signatures
- CATP assessment results

---

### 5.2 **Requirement 12.3.3: Cryptographic Cipher Suite & Protocol Reviews (Annual)**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 had general cryptography requirements without explicit review frequency
- v4.0.1 **mandates annual review** of cryptographic algorithms and protocols in use

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- At minimum annually, review all cryptographic cipher suites, algorithms, and protocols in use for CDE data protection:
  - Encryption algorithms (TLS/SSL, IPsec, AES, etc.)
  - Key exchange protocols
  - Authentication mechanisms
  - Hash algorithms (SHA-1 deprecated; SHA-256+ required)
  - Any legacy or weak cryptography
- Assess against industry standards (NIST, OWASP, TLS 1.2+)
- Document findings and plan remediation for weak/deprecated algorithms
- Track cipher suite configuration changes and updates

**Implementation Priority:** MEDIUM
**Common Gaps:** Annual review not documented; legacy protocols (TLS 1.0/1.1) still in use; no cipher suite inventory; weak algorithms not identified; no remediation planning

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Documented annual cryptography review (at least 1 completed in past 12 months)
- Inventory of cipher suites and protocols in use
- Assessment against standards (NIST, TLS 1.2 minimum)
- Remediation plan for weak/deprecated algorithms

---

### 5.3 **Requirement 12.3.4: Hardware & Software Technology Reviews (Annual)**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 did not explicitly mandate technology lifecycle reviews
- v4.0.1 **mandates annual review** of hardware and software technologies in the CDE

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- At minimum annually, review hardware and software technologies used for CDE protection:
  - Operating systems (vendor support/patch status)
  - Database systems
  - Security appliances (firewalls, IDS/IPS)
  - Anti-malware, encryption, and authentication solutions
  - Protocol versions (identify end-of-life components)
- Document review findings:
  - Technology inventory
  - Support/patch status
  - End-of-life dates
  - Upgrade/replacement plans
- Track technology updates and retirements

**Implementation Priority:** MEDIUM
**Common Gaps:** No annual technology reviews; legacy/unsupported software in CDE (Windows XP, deprecated databases); no patch/update tracking; technology inventory missing; no lifecycle planning

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Technology inventory and review documentation
- Support/patch status assessment
- End-of-life tracking and upgrade plans
- Update/upgrade execution records

---

### 5.4 **Requirement 12.8.4: Third-Party Service Provider (TPSP) Compliance Status Monitoring**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 required TPSP compliance but monitoring frequency was less explicit
- v4.0.1 **mandates at least annual monitoring** of TPSP compliance status

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- For each Third-Party Service Provider (TPSP) with access to CDE or handling CHD:
  - At minimum annually, verify and document TPSP PCI DSS compliance status
  - Obtain current Attestation of Compliance (AOC) or Report on Compliance (ROC) from TPSP
  - Review compliance status and any noted exceptions/compensating controls
  - Maintain TPSP register documenting compliance verification dates
  - Address any non-compliance findings or missing evidence
  - Include TPSP compliance assessment in annual PCI DSS validation

**Implementation Priority:** HIGH (if using TPSPs)
**Common Gaps:** TPSP compliance verified only at initial vendor selection; no annual re-verification; outdated AOC/ROC; no TPSP register maintained; gaps in TPSP coverage not identified

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- TPSP register with compliance verification dates
- Current AOC/ROC documentation for all in-scope TPSPs
- Compliance review records and findings
- Evidence of addressing TPSP compliance gaps

---

### 5.5 **Requirement 12.9.2: TPSP Support for Compliance Responsibility Confirmation**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 had TPSP management requirements
- v4.0.1 **explicitly requires TPSPs to support customer confirmation** of responsibility for compliance

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- When engaging a TPSP, require contractually that the TPSP will:
  - Provide clear documentation of what PCI DSS requirements they are responsible for meeting
  - Provide confirmation of their compliance status (AOC/ROC)
  - Support the customer's (your) PCI DSS compliance assessment and validation
  - Clarify responsibility boundaries (which party is responsible for which control)
- Document TPSP responsibility matrix as part of your PCI DSS compliance program
- Ensure TPSP contracts include PCI DSS compliance clauses and responsibility definitions

**Implementation Priority:** MEDIUM
**Common Gaps:** TPSP contracts lack PCI DSS clauses; unclear responsibility boundaries; TPSPs unwilling to provide AOC/ROC; no documented compliance responsibility matrix

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- TPSP contracts with PCI DSS compliance clauses
- TPSP responsibility documentation (responsibility matrix)
- TPSP AOC/ROC and compliance confirmation letters
- Evidence of responsibility alignment with TPSP

---

### 5.6 **Requirement 12.10.4.1: Incident Response Personnel Training (Annual)**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 required incident response plan but did not explicitly mandate IR personnel training frequency
- v4.0.1 **mandates minimum annual training** for incident response personnel

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- Identify incident response team members (IR coordinator, forensics staff, management, communications leads, technical leads)
- Provide PCI DSS-focused incident response training at minimum every 12 months covering:
  - Incident detection and escalation procedures
  - Data breach response (cardholder data discovery, notification, remediation)
  - Forensic evidence preservation
  - Notification requirements and timelines
  - Post-incident review and lessons learned
  - Role-specific responsibilities
- Document training completion and attendance
- Update training materials annually to reflect evolving threats and PCI DSS changes

**Implementation Priority:** MEDIUM
**Common Gaps:** IR plan exists but no personnel training; training infrequent (not annual); training content minimal; no documentation of training completion; new team members not trained

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- IR personnel list and training records
- Training curriculum documentation
- Training completion records (dates, attendees)
- Evidence of annual training delivery

---

### 5.7 **Requirement 12.10.7: Incident Response Procedures for Unexpected PAN Discovery**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 did not explicitly require procedures for accidental PAN discovery
- v4.0.1 **mandates documented procedures** for discovery of stored PAN in unexpected locations

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- Develop and document incident response procedures specifically for discovering PAN data in unexpected locations (e.g., unencrypted logs, backup files, test systems, developer workstations)
- Procedures must include:
  - Immediate notification and escalation pathway
  - Data containment and isolation steps
  - Investigation procedures (determine scope, retention duration, how it occurred)
  - Remediation (delete data, correct control failures)
  - Risk assessment (determine if breach notification required)
  - Documentation and reporting to management
  - Prevention measures to prevent recurrence
- Test procedures at least once to confirm effectiveness

**Implementation Priority:** MEDIUM
**Common Gaps:** No specific procedure for unexpected PAN discovery; general incident response procedures too broad; procedures not tested; no established escalation pathway; remediation steps unclear

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Documented incident response procedure for PAN discovery
- Evidence of procedure testing or past incident response execution
- Management and board awareness of procedure
- Incident response records if discovery has occurred

---

### 5.8 **Requirement 12.5.2.1: PCI DSS Scope Verification (Service Providers — Every 6 Months)**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 required scope verification but frequency was less explicit for multi-tenant service providers
- v4.0.1 **mandates at least 6-month scope verification** for multi-tenant service providers

**What Must Be Implemented (if applicable):**
**Applies to: Multi-tenant service providers hosting systems for multiple customers**

- At minimum every 6 months, formally verify PCI DSS scope:
  - Confirm which customer systems/environments are in scope for PCI DSS
  - Verify data isolation between customers
  - Confirm CHD/SAD handling requirements
  - Document any scope changes since last verification
  - Communicate scope changes to affected customers
- Maintain documented scope verification records for audit/assessment

**Implementation Priority:** MEDIUM (if multi-tenant service provider)
**Common Gaps:** Scope verification done infrequently; no documented records; new customers not properly scoped; scope creep not detected; customers unaware of their scope

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Documented scope verification schedule and records (at least 2 verifications in past 12 months)
- Scope documentation for each customer environment
- Scope change records and customer notifications
- Data isolation verification and testing

---

## Section 6: Monitoring & Security Testing

### 6.1 **Requirement 10.7.2: Critical Security Control Failure Detection & Reporting**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 had general log review requirements
- v4.0.1 **mandates proactive detection and prompt reporting** of critical security control failures

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- Implement mechanisms (automated or manual review) to detect when critical security controls fail:
  - Firewall rule violations or disabled firewall
  - Anti-malware disabled or unsuccessful scans
  - Encryption failures or disabled encryption
  - Access control failures (unauthorized access attempts)
  - Authentication/MFA failures or bypass
  - Log deletion or tampering
  - Backup failures
- Critical control failures must be detected and reported promptly to appropriate personnel
- Logging of detection and response actions
- Investigation and remediation of failures

**Implementation Priority:** HIGH
**Common Gaps:** Automated detection not configured; failures detected but not reported; no escalation process; delayed response to control failures; no investigation procedure

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- SIEM/monitoring rules for critical control failures
- Alert notification logs showing detected failures
- Incident response records and remediation evidence
- Escalation procedures and confirmation of notification

---

### 6.2 **Requirement 10.7.3: Response Timeframe for Critical Control Failures**

**Change from v3.2.1:**
- v3.2.1 did not explicitly mandate response timeframe for control failures
- v4.0.1 **requires response within defined timeframes** to critical security control failures

**What Must Be Implemented:**
- Define response timeframes (via Targeted Risk Analysis) for each type of critical security control failure
- Document response procedures and escalation pathways
- Respond to critical control failures within defined timeframes (typically immediate to within hours, depending on criticality)
- Document all responses and remediation actions
- Verify control restoration after remediation

**Implementation Priority:** HIGH
**Common Gaps:** No defined response timeframes; delays in responding to control failures; no escalation procedures; failures not remediated promptly; no documentation of responses

**Evidence Required by QSA:**
- Documented response timeframes for critical control failures (TRA)
- Incident response procedure and escalation chart
- Incident response logs showing detection, response, and remediation times
- Evidence of control restoration

---

## Section 7: Scope & Multi-Tenant Environment Management

### 7.1 **Requirement 4.2.1.1: Trusted Certificate/Key Inventory Management**

*(Note: This requirement was covered above in Section 4.2)*

## Key Implementation Recommendations for QSA Assessment Preparation

### Immediate Priority Actions (Pre-Assessment):

1. **MFA Deployment (Req 8.4.2)**
   - Audit current MFA coverage — identify all non-MFA CDE access points
   - Extend MFA to all CDE systems, including internal network access
   - Validate MFA enforcement and confirm fallback mechanisms
   - Timeline: Complete before assessment

2. **Payment Page Script Security (Req 6.4.3 & 11.6.1)** *(if e-commerce)*
   - Conduct complete payment page script inventory
   - Document business justification for each script
   - Deploy CSP/SRI headers or equivalent integrity controls
   - Implement automated tamper detection
   - Timeline: Complete before assessment

3. **Automated Log Review (Req 10.4.1.1)**
   - Implement or upgrade SIEM/log management system
   - Configure automated alert rules for security events
   - Test alerting and response procedures
   - Timeline: Complete before assessment

4. **Access Control Reviews (Req 7.2.4, 7.2.5)**
   - Conduct current-state account access review (if not done in past 6 months)
   - Inventory all system/application accounts
   - Document access privilege basis
   - Timeline: Complete before assessment

5. **Third-Party Service Provider Compliance (Req 12.8.4)**
   - Update TPSP register with current compliance status
   - Obtain latest AOC/ROC from all in-scope TPSPs
   - Document compliance monitoring procedures
   - Timeline: Complete before assessment

### Medium-Term Implementation (Next 3-6 months):

- Hardcoded credential scanning and remediation (Req 8.6.2)
- Software Bill of Materials (SBOM) creation for custom software (Req 6.3.2)
- Annual reviews documentation: cryptography (Req 12.3.3), technology (Req 12.3.4)
- Incident response training and procedure documentation (Req 12.10.4.1, 12.10.7)

---

## Assessment Readiness Checklist

When your QSA arrives for assessment, prepare evidence for these 22 new mandatory requirements:

- [ ] MFA deployment configuration and enforcement logs
- [ ] Payment page script inventory and authorization documentation (if applicable)
- [ ] Tamper detection system configuration and alerting logs (if applicable)
- [ ] SIEM automated log review configuration and alert logs
- [ ] Documented 6-month access privilege review (completed within 12 months, at least 2 cycles)
- [ ] System/application account inventory with business justification
- [ ] Code repository scanning results confirming no hardcoded credentials
- [ ] Software Bill of Materials for custom software (if applicable)
- [ ] Anti-phishing solution configuration and detection logs
- [ ] Annual cryptographic algorithm and protocol review documentation
- [ ] Annual technology lifecycle review documentation
- [ ] TPSP register with current compliance verification dates and AOC/ROC
- [ ] TPSP responsibility matrix documentation
- [ ] Incident response personnel training records (annual)
- [ ] Incident response procedures for unexpected PAN discovery
- [ ] Critical control failure detection and response procedures
- [ ] Targeted Risk Analysis documentation (if using Customised Approach)
- [ ] Key/certificate inventory documentation

---

## Conclusion

The transition from PCI DSS v3.2.1 to v4.0.1 introduced **22 significant new mandatory requirements** effective March 31, 2025. These changes reflect evolving security threats, with particular emphasis on:

- **Authentication security** (MFA expansion to all CDE access)
- **Payment page security** (web skimming prevention via script controls and tamper detection)
- **Logging and monitoring automation** (shift from manual review to automated detection)
- **Software security** (SBOM, hardcoded credential elimination)
- **Governance and periodic reviews** (TRA, annual technology assessments)
- **Third-party risk management** (enhanced TPSP monitoring and responsibility clarity)

Organizations that passed v3.2.1 assessments prior to March 31, 2024, should expect their next PCI DSS assessment (v4.0.1) to require evidence of these new controls. Gaps in any of these requirements will be documented as findings and must be remediated before passing validation.

**Begin implementation immediately to ensure readiness for your next QSA assessment.**

# PCI DSS v4.0.1: Key Mandatory Requirements (Effective March 31, 2025)

## Overview
PCI DSS v4.0.1 represents a significant evolution from v3.2.1, introducing more prescriptive security controls and emphasizing risk-based approaches. The following are the most important new requirements that became mandatory on March 31, 2025.

## 1. Requirement 1: Network Security

### 1.4.7 - Firewall Configuration Review
**Change:** New requirement mandating documented review and approval of all firewall rules at least every six months.
- Organizations must maintain documentation of all firewall rules
- Rules must be reviewed and approved by appropriate personnel
- Any unnecessary or deprecated rules must be removed
- This replaces the more general firewall documentation requirements from v3.2.1

### 1.5.1 - Stateful Firewall Technology
**Change:** Enhanced requirement for stateful packet inspection firewalls on network boundaries.
- All connections between the internet and cardholder data environment must use stateful firewalls
- Firewalls must be configured to perform stateful inspection
- This is more prescriptive than the v3.2.1 requirement

## 2. Requirement 2: Default Passwords and Security Parameters

### 2.4 - Configuration Documentation
**Change:** Significantly expanded requirements for configuration documentation.
- All hardware must be documented with inventory and configuration details
- Default credentials must be changed for all devices
- Justification required for any defaults that cannot be changed
- Configuration baselines must be established and maintained
- This is more detailed than the v3.2.1 approach

## 3. Requirement 3: Protect Stored Cardholder Data

### 3.2.1 - Data Minimization
**Change:** New requirement limiting storage of sensitive authentication data.
- Magnetic stripe, CVV/CVC, PIN, and other sensitive authentication data cannot be stored (strengthened requirement)
- Organizations must implement controls to prevent storage of this data
- Compensating controls are not permitted for this requirement

### 3.4 - Rendering PAN Unreadable
**Change:** Enhanced encryption and masking requirements.
- One-way hashes must be generated using a strong cryptographic function
- Key rotation procedures must be documented and followed
- For key management: at least two different methods must be used together
- Third-party key management solutions may be used but with documented responsibility allocation

## 4. Requirement 4: Protect Cardholder Data in Transit

### 4.1 - Encryption of Transmissions
**Change:** Expanded use of TLS/SSL requirements.
- All connections transmitting cardholder data must use strong cryptography
- TLS 1.2 or higher is now the minimum requirement
- Weak ciphers and protocols must be explicitly disabled
- Certificate management requirements are more prescriptive
- Compensation controls for SSL/early TLS no longer permitted

## 5. Requirement 5: Protect Systems Against Malware

### 5.1 - Malware Protection
**Change:** Expanded anti-malware requirements.
- All systems must have malware protection (not just cardholder data systems)
- Anti-malware solutions must be kept current
- Automated tools are now required (not recommended)
- Real-time scanning and logging are mandatory
- Regular scans must be scheduled and results reviewed

## 6. Requirement 6: Secure Development and Vulnerability Management

### 6.2 - Security Patches
**Change:** Significantly tightened patch management timelines.
- Critical security patches must be installed within 30 days of release
- This is a substantial change from v3.2.1 which allowed more time
- All patches, including OS and application patches, are included
- Compensating controls are not permitted for critical patches

### 6.3.2 - Custom Code Changes
**Change:** New requirement for change control processes.
- All changes to custom code must follow documented change control procedures
- Changes must be tested before implementation
- Code review requirements are more specific
- Security testing of code changes is now mandatory

### 6.4 - Public-Facing Web Applications
**Change:** Enhanced vulnerability management requirements.
- Regular vulnerability scans are now required (not optional)
- Web application firewalls are specifically recommended
- Code reviews are mandatory for public-facing web applications
- Penetration testing must include web applications

### 6.5.4 - Insecure Cryptographic Storage
**Change:** More prescriptive requirements for cryptographic implementations.
- Broken or at-risk cryptographic algorithms must not be used
- Approved cryptographic standards must be documented
- Key management procedures must address algorithm deprecation

## 7. Requirement 7: Restrict Access to Cardholder Data

### 7.1 - Principle of Least Privilege
**Change:** Enhanced access control requirements.
- Access must be limited based on documented business need
- Default "deny all" access policy is now required
- Role-based access control (RBAC) is specifically recommended
- Access reviews must be performed at least annually (quarterly for certain roles)

### 7.2 - Access Control Implementation
**Change:** More specific technical requirements.
- Multi-factor authentication (MFA) is now required for all non-console administrative access
- Compensating controls for MFA are extremely limited
- Service accounts must have documented purposes and restrictions
- Privileged access must be logged and monitored

## 8. Requirement 8: Identify and Authenticate Access

### 8.1.1 - User Identification
**Change:** Enhanced requirements for authentication mechanisms.
- Unique user IDs are mandatory for all system access
- Session tokens must be generated by secure systems
- Session management must prevent token reuse

### 8.2.3 - MFA Requirements
**Change:** Expanded multi-factor authentication mandate.
- MFA is required for all administrative access (including console)
- Compensating controls for MFA are not permitted
- MFA must be implemented using two or more different authentication types
- This represents a major expansion from v3.2.1

### 8.3 - Password Strength
**Change:** Enhanced password complexity requirements.
- Minimum 8 characters is the baseline (previously 7)
- Complexity requirements are more detailed
- For certain roles, 12+ character passwords are required
- Passphrases are now explicitly permitted as alternatives

### 8.5 - Access Control and Password Management
**Change:** New requirements for password management.
- Password management systems must be used for all administrative accounts
- Hard-coded passwords in applications are prohibited
- Password storage must follow encryption requirements

## 9. Requirement 9: Restrict Physical Access

### 9.1 - Physical Access Controls
**Change:** More comprehensive physical security requirements.
- All facilities housing cardholder data systems must have controlled access
- Physical barriers and entry points must be documented
- Visitor access procedures must be formalized
- Badge and key management procedures must be documented

### 9.4 - Physical Media Logging
**Change:** New requirement for tracking physical assets.
- Movement of physical storage media must be logged
- Database of media must be maintained
- Media must be tracked through its lifecycle

## 10. Requirement 10: Track and Monitor Network Access

### 10.2 - Log Requirements
**Change:** Expanded logging and monitoring requirements.
- Logs must capture user identification for all access to cardholder data
- All administrative actions must be logged
- All access to cardholder data must be logged (not just attempts)
- Failed access attempts must be logged separately

### 10.3 - Log Protection
**Change:** Enhanced log security requirements.
- Logs must be protected from unauthorized access, modification, and deletion
- Log file integrity checking must be implemented
- Centralized log management is now strongly recommended
- Real-time monitoring and alerting are required

### 10.4.3 - Log Review
**Change:** More specific log review procedures.
- Logs must be reviewed at least daily
- Procedures must define roles and responsibilities for log review
- Automated alerting for suspicious activity is required
- Security team must review logs for indicators of compromise

## 11. Requirement 11: Regularly Test Security Systems

### 11.3 - Penetration Testing
**Change:** Enhanced penetration testing requirements.
- Internal penetration testing is now required annually
- External penetration testing scope expanded to include web applications
- Testing must be performed by qualified external parties
- Findings must be remediated and retesting performed

### 11.4 - External Vulnerability Scanning
**Change:** More specific scanning requirements.
- Quarterly scans by approved scanning vendors are required
- Failed scans must be rerun within 30 days
- Scan scope must include all systems in the CDE
- Scan results must be reviewed and signed by technical staff

### 11.5 - Change Detection
**Change:** New requirement for file integrity monitoring.
- File integrity monitoring tools must be implemented
- Critical system files must be monitored
- Unauthorized changes must trigger alerts
- Compensating controls for this requirement are not permitted

## 12. Requirement 12: Maintain Information Security Policy

### 12.2 - Risk Assessment
**Change:** More comprehensive risk assessment requirements.
- Risk assessments must identify all systems and data flows
- Threat modeling must be performed
- Assessment results must be documented and approved by management
- Annual updates are required at minimum

### 12.3.10 - Incident Response Plan
**Change:** Enhanced incident response requirements.
- Incident response procedures must be documented and tested
- Forensic procedures must be included
- Procedures for evidence preservation must be detailed
- Testing and tabletop exercises are now required

### 12.6.2 - Security Training
**Change:** Enhanced security awareness requirements.
- All personnel must receive annual security training
- Training must be documented with completion tracking
- Role-specific training is required for technical staff
- Training must cover PCI DSS and incident response procedures

## Key Transition Points from v3.2.1 to v4.0.1

### Removed Compensating Controls Options
- MFA requirements now have very limited compensating controls
- Patch management compensating controls for critical patches are not permitted
- File integrity monitoring has no compensating controls
- These changes represent a shift toward more mandatory, prescriptive security

### Strengthened Cryptography Requirements
- Deprecated cryptographic algorithms (like SHA-1) are explicitly prohibited
- Key management procedures are more detailed
- Encryption is required for more data states (at-rest and in-transit)

### Expanded Risk-Based Approach
- Documentation of risk-based decisions is more rigorous
- Annual risk assessments are mandatory
- Threat modeling and risk prioritization are required elements

### Enhanced Logging and Monitoring
- Real-time alerting is now required (not optional)
- Centralized log management is strongly recommended
- Log retention periods are more specific

### Increased Focus on Supplier and Third-Party Risk
- Service provider assessments must be more comprehensive
- Documentation of third-party security assessments is required
- Contractual security requirements are more specific

## Implementation Priorities

For organizations that were previously compliant with v3.2.1, the recommended implementation priorities for v4.0.1 compliance include:

1. **Immediate (Month 1):** MFA implementation for all administrative access, password policy updates, and critical patch management procedures
2. **Short-term (Months 2-3):** File integrity monitoring, enhanced logging and monitoring infrastructure, and access control review procedures
3. **Medium-term (Months 4-6):** Risk assessment updates, vulnerability management program enhancements, and supplier management documentation
4. **Ongoing:** Training, testing, and continuous monitoring implementation

## Conclusion

PCI DSS v4.0.1 represents a significant tightening of security requirements, with particular emphasis on:
- Mandatory strong authentication and authorization controls
- More prescriptive vulnerability management and patch procedures
- Enhanced monitoring, logging, and incident response capabilities
- Reduced reliance on compensating controls for critical security areas
- More comprehensive risk management and documentation requirements

Organizations must systematically review each requirement against their current v3.2.1 implementation and prioritize the most impactful changes for timely compliance.

# TSA Cybersecurity Compliance Assessment
## TransGulf Pipeline LLC — Natural Gas Transmission Pipeline

**Assessment Date:** March 25, 2026
**Company:** TransGulf Pipeline LLC
**Sector:** Natural Gas Transmission Pipeline (Texas)
**Compliance Status:** Not Yet Started

---

## Executive Summary

TransGulf Pipeline LLC, as a natural gas transmission pipeline operator, **is subject to TSA Security Directive requirements** if TSA has designated your facilities as critical infrastructure. This assessment outlines the directives that apply to you, your immediate obligations, and the core compliance documents you must create and submit to TSA.

**Key Takeaway:** TSA designates covered entities individually. You should immediately confirm with TSA whether your specific facilities are designated as critical — if so, your compliance obligations are urgent and structured in phases.

---

## Part 1: Which Security Directives Apply to You

### Applicable Directive Series: SD Pipeline-2021

As a natural gas transmission pipeline company, you fall under the **SD Pipeline-2021 series**, which consists of two primary directives:

| Directive | Current Revision | Effective Date | Scope & Focus |
|-----------|------------------|-----------------|---------------|
| **SD Pipeline-2021-01** | G | January 2026 | **Immediate measures:** Cybersecurity incident reporting to CISA; designation of a Cybersecurity Coordinator; baseline review of current cybersecurity practices |
| **SD Pipeline-2021-02** | F | Rolling (latest) | **Comprehensive program:** Full Cyber Risk Management Program (CRMP) with CIP/COIP, Incident Response Plan, Architecture Design Review, and Cybersecurity Assessment Plan |

### Coverage Criteria

You are covered if:
- Your organisation owns or operates a **hazardous liquid or natural gas pipeline facility** (including transmission, distribution, or LNG)
- **TSA has designated your facility as critical** based on factors including volume of throughput, proximity to population centres, and national security impact
- You have received formal notification from TSA

**Important:** Not all pipeline operators are subject to mandatory TSA directives. Designations are risk-based and individualized. **If you have not yet received formal TSA notification that you are a covered entity, your first action should be to contact TSA to confirm your designation status.**

---

## Part 2: Immediate Obligations (SD Pipeline-2021-01)

If TSA has designated you as a covered entity, the following **immediate obligations** from SD Pipeline-2021-01 (Revision G) apply:

### 1. Establish Cybersecurity Incident Reporting Capability

**Requirement:** Your organisation must be prepared to report cybersecurity incidents to CISA within **24 hours** of identification.

**What must be reported:**
- Unauthorised access to any Critical Cyber System (CCS) — both IT and OT systems
- Discovery or suspected presence of malware, ransomware, or other malicious code on CCS
- Denial-of-service events affecting operational capability
- Confirmed phishing or social engineering attacks that resulted in system access
- Any incident reasonably likely to result in operational disruption or loss of control

**Reporting contacts:**
- **CISA 24/7 Operations Center:** 1-888-282-0870
- **Email:** CISAgov@mail.dhs.gov
- **Note:** You must also notify TSA in parallel with CISA

**Critical guidance:** Do **not** delay reporting while internal investigation continues. Provide your initial report based on available information; updates follow as the investigation matures. Delayed reporting (after the 24-hour window) must include a documented explanation of why reporting was delayed.

### 2. Designate a Cybersecurity Coordinator

**Requirement:** You must designate a primary Cybersecurity Coordinator and a backup designee and submit their contact information to TSA via the designated TSA reporting system.

**Coordinator qualifications and responsibilities:**
- Must be available **24 hours a day, 7 days a week** (or have a backup designee who is)
- Serves as the primary point of contact between your organisation, TSA, and CISA for all cybersecurity matters
- Coordinates your organisation's response to cybersecurity incidents
- Ensures incident reports are submitted to CISA within the 24-hour window
- Maintains awareness of your organisation's Critical Cyber Systems inventory
- Oversees implementation of cybersecurity measures and plans
- Must have sufficient authority and organisational access to execute these duties

**Submission:** Provide the Cybersecurity Coordinator's name, title, email, and 24/7 phone number to TSA in writing.

### 3. Conduct Baseline Cybersecurity Practices Review

**Requirement:** Conduct a comprehensive review of your current cybersecurity practices, systems, and policies to establish a baseline and identify gaps.

**Scope of the review:**
- Identify all systems that qualify as Critical Cyber Systems (CCS) — systems whose compromise could result in operational disruption, safety impact, environmental impact, or national security impact
- Document your current IT and OT network architecture
- Assess current access control practices (user accounts, authentication, privilege management)
- Evaluate current monitoring and logging capabilities
- Review patch management and change control processes
- Assess incident detection and response capabilities
- Identify any existing cybersecurity policies, tools, and personnel

**Output:** Document findings in a **Baseline Cybersecurity Practices Review Report** that identifies:
- Current state of cybersecurity measures
- Gaps relative to expected TSA requirements
- Prioritised remediation roadmap

This baseline report will inform your Cybersecurity Implementation Plan (CIP) and guide your path forward under SD Pipeline-2021-02.

---

## Part 3: Comprehensive Compliance Program (SD Pipeline-2021-02)

SD Pipeline-2021-02 (Revision F) requires implementation of a comprehensive **Cyber Risk Management Program (CRMP)** with four core components. You must begin work on these immediately; they form the foundation of your compliance effort.

### Component 1: Cybersecurity Implementation Plan (CIP) or Cybersecurity Operational Implementation Plan (COIP)

**What it is:** A formal plan submitted to TSA that describes how your organisation will meet all CRMP requirements. This is your primary compliance document.

**Required sections:**
1. **Executive Summary** — Overview of your cybersecurity program and commitment to compliance
2. **Leadership Structure** — Identification of the Accountable Executive (C-suite) and Cybersecurity Coordinator; roles and responsibilities
3. **Critical Cyber Systems Inventory** — Complete inventory of systems whose compromise could disrupt operations, affect safety, or impact national security
4. **Network Architecture Description** — Current IT and OT network topology; segmentation mechanisms; communication flows; remote access paths; third-party/vendor connectivity
5. **Baseline Cybersecurity Measures** — Description of how each of the four technical security domains (see below) is implemented:
   - Network segmentation (IT/OT separation)
   - Access controls (authentication, MFA, privilege management)
   - Continuous monitoring and anomaly detection
   - Patch management and vulnerability remediation
6. **Protective Measures** — Specific policies and technical controls for each domain
7. **Incident Detection Procedures** — How your organisation identifies cybersecurity threats and anomalies
8. **Incident Response Procedures** — Step-by-step process for detecting, containing, remediating, and reporting incidents
9. **Annual Review Process** — How the CIP is kept current as systems and threats evolve
10. **Change Management** — Process for updating the CIP when systems or controls change

**CIP Approval:** TSA reviews your submitted CIP and either:
- **Approves** — You may use the CIP as compliance evidence
- **Requests Modifications** — You revise and resubmit
- **Rejects** — You develop a new CIP and resubmit

**Difference between CIP and COIP:** A COIP (Cybersecurity Operational Implementation Plan) is the CIP tailored for operational implementation — some organisations develop both; others use the CIP as the operational document. Clarify with TSA which your organisation should submit.

### Component 2: Incident Response Plan (IRP)

**What it is:** A detailed, documented plan for detecting, responding to, and recovering from cybersecurity incidents affecting Critical Cyber Systems.

**Required elements:**
1. **Roles and Responsibilities** — Who is involved in incident response (Cybersecurity Coordinator, operations staff, IT/OT teams, leadership, external parties)
2. **Detection and Analysis Procedures** — How incidents are identified; what triggers an incident response
3. **Containment Procedures** — How to isolate affected systems and prevent further compromise
4. **Eradication and Recovery Procedures** — How to remove malware/attacker access and restore normal operations
5. **Communication Procedures** — Internal escalation; notification to CISA/TSA; leadership briefing; employee communication
6. **Post-Incident Review Process** — How lessons learned are captured and improvements implemented
7. **Third-Party Coordination** — How you coordinate with OT vendors, IT vendors, and service providers during incidents

**Critical requirement:** You must **test at least two objectives of your IRP annually**. Testing objectives typically include:
- IT/OT segregation testing (can OT operate safely if IT is compromised?)
- Backup and recovery testing (can you restore critical data and systems?)
- Ransomware containment scenario (can you detect and isolate a ransomware infection?)
- Communication and escalation procedures (do incident response staff know how to escalate and report?)

**Evidence retention:** Keep documented evidence of testing (date, scenario, participants, findings, corrective actions taken).

### Component 3: Architecture Design Review (ADR)

**What it is:** An annual structured assessment of your IT and OT network architecture to identify vulnerabilities, segmentation gaps, and unauthorised connections.

**ADR scope:**
1. **Network topology review** — Obtain current, accurate diagrams of your IT network, OT network, DMZs, firewalls, and interconnections
2. **IT/OT segmentation assessment** — Evaluate the effectiveness of boundary controls (firewalls, DMZs, data diodes, unidirectional gateways)
3. **Unauthorised connections** — Identify any undocumented or unplanned network connections to CCS (e.g., direct connections, bypasses, wireless access)
4. **Remote access paths** — Map all remote access into OT environments; assess security controls on jump servers and VPN gateways
5. **Third-party connectivity** — Document all vendor/service provider network connections to CCS and assess security controls
6. **Vulnerability scan integration** — Review results of network vulnerability scans and assess risk
7. **Findings and remediation** — Document gaps and create a prioritised remediation action plan with target timelines

**Output:** Updated network diagrams; findings report; remediation action plan; evidence of review and approval by leadership.

**Frequency:** At minimum annually; more frequently if architecture changes significantly.

### Component 4: Cybersecurity Assessment Plan (CAP)

**What it is:** A formal plan describing how your organisation will assess the effectiveness of its CRMP annually. This includes vulnerability assessments, penetration testing, and compliance reviews.

**Required CAP elements:**
1. **Scope** — Which CCS, systems, and CRMP components are included in the annual assessment
2. **Assessment Methodology** — Specific techniques:
   - Network vulnerability scanning
   - Penetration testing (external and internal)
   - Configuration review and hardening assessment
   - Access control testing
   - Incident response procedure testing
   - Policy and process review
3. **Assessment Schedule** — Timeline for assessments throughout the year (e.g., Q1 network scan, Q2 penetration test, Q3 policy review)
4. **Responsible Parties** — Internal staff or third-party assessors conducting assessments
5. **Reporting Requirements** — How results are documented, reviewed, and reported to TSA annually

**Annual submission:** You must submit CAP results to TSA, including:
- Findings and vulnerabilities identified
- Remediation status for previously identified issues
- Open vulnerabilities and timeline for remediation
- Evidence of assessment execution

---

## Part 4: Four Technical Security Domains

These are the specific technical cybersecurity measures you must implement and maintain across all CCS:

### Domain 1: Network Segmentation

**Objective:** Develop and enforce a clear boundary between your corporate IT network and your operational technology (OT) network such that if one is compromised, the other can continue to operate safely.

**Implementation requirements:**
- Develop a formal **Network Segmentation Policy** that defines the IT/OT boundary
- Implement **firewalls, DMZs, or physical separation** between IT and OT networks
- Ensure **no direct routable connections** between corporate IT and OT/ICS networks without explicit security controls
- All **remote access to OT must be routed through a DMZ or jump server** with multi-factor authentication
- Document and maintain a list of all **segmentation exceptions** with business justification; review exceptions at least annually
- Test segmentation effectiveness at least annually (via IRP testing or ADR)

**Evidence to retain for TSA audits:**
- Current, accurate network topology diagrams
- Firewall ruleset documentation and ACL reviews
- Segmentation testing results and reports
- Policy documents and exception approvals

**OT-specific considerations:**
- Passive network monitoring is preferred for OT (active scanning can disrupt industrial protocols)
- Be aware of legacy equipment that may not support modern network controls (e.g., older PLCs)
- Implement compensating controls for unpatchable or hard-to-isolate systems

### Domain 2: Access Controls

**Objective:** Implement controls to prevent unauthorised access to Critical Cyber Systems, ensuring only authorised users with a legitimate business need can access each system.

**Implementation requirements:**
- **Unique user accounts** — No shared accounts on any CCS; each user must have a unique account
- **Multi-factor authentication (MFA)** — Required for all remote access to CCS and all privileged access to CCS
- **Principle of least privilege** — Each user account has only the minimum permissions needed for their role
- **Privileged Access Management (PAM)** — OT administrator accounts should be managed through a PAM solution with session recording and approval workflows
- **Access reviews** — Conduct formal access reviews at least annually to ensure accounts remain appropriate and inactive accounts are removed
- **Vendor/third-party access** — Any remote access by vendors or service providers must be time-limited, monitored, and approved
- **Immediate revocation** — When users leave the organisation or change roles, access must be revoked immediately

**Evidence to retain:**
- Documented access control policy
- Account inventory and system access matrix
- MFA deployment configuration and reports
- PAM solution configuration and session logs
- Access review records and audit trails

### Domain 3: Continuous Monitoring and Anomaly Detection

**Objective:** Implement systems and processes to continuously monitor CCS for cybersecurity threats and detect anomalous behaviour indicating a potential compromise.

**Implementation requirements:**
- **OT-specific monitoring tools** — Deploy tools designed for OT environments (not just IT-focused SIEM); examples include Claroty, Dragos, Nozomi Networks, Armis, or Microsoft Defender for IoT
- **Network visibility** — Establish visibility into OT network communications; understand what protocols and endpoints are normal
- **Baseline establishment** — Define what normal OT network behaviour looks like (typical protocols, communication frequency, endpoints)
- **Anomaly detection** — Configure detection for deviations from the baseline (unusual protocols, unauthorised endpoints, unexpected data flows)
- **Log collection** — Collect and retain logs from CCS, firewalls, and security tools
- **Alerting and escalation** — When anomalies are detected, alert security staff and define escalation procedures
- **Remote access monitoring** — Log and monitor all remote access sessions to CCS; review logs regularly
- **SOC integration** — Integrate with your Security Operations Centre (SOC) or define escalation path to security analysts

**OT monitoring considerations:**
- Passive monitoring is strongly preferred (active scanning can interfere with industrial processes)
- Build a baseline of normal OT traffic before deploying anomaly detection (prevents false positives)
- Focus detection on indicators of compromise: lateral movement, credential abuse, unusual protocol usage, unauthorised devices

### Domain 4: Patch Management

**Objective:** Identify vulnerabilities in systems supporting CCS and apply security patches and updates in a timely, risk-based manner.

**Implementation requirements:**
- **Patch management policy** — Formal policy defining patch SLAs (Service Level Agreements) for different severity levels
- **Risk-based prioritisation** — Critical and high-severity vulnerabilities patched faster than medium and low; define SLAs (e.g., critical patches within 30 days)
- **OT patch process** — Unique workflow for OT systems:
  - Obtain vendor approval before patching (to avoid voiding warranties or disrupting systems)
  - Test patches in a non-production environment before deploying to production
  - Plan patches during maintenance windows (may be quarterly or annual for some OT systems)
- **Compensating controls for legacy systems** — For unpatchable legacy OT equipment (old PLCs, RTUs), implement network isolation and enhanced monitoring as compensating controls
- **Vulnerability scanning** — Regularly scan CCS to identify unpatched vulnerabilities; define frequency (at least quarterly recommended)
- **Exception process** — Document exceptions for patches requiring extended downtime; provide business justification and alternative controls

**OT patching realities:**
- Many OT systems have limited patch windows (e.g., annual or quarterly maintenance)
- Vendor approval is often required and can be slow
- Some legacy equipment is no longer patchable; compensating controls are essential
- Testing is critical — OT patches can have unintended consequences on industrial processes

---

   - Contact information for your Cybersecurity Coordinator
